Central Indonesia Junior Secondary Education Project
LOAN AND PROJECT SUMMARY
Borrower:                     Republic of Indonesia
Implementing Agency:          Directorate General of Primary and Secondary Education
(DGPSE), Ministry of Education and Culture
Beneficiaries:                School children and out-of-school youth , particularly those
in rural communities and girls, in the Provinces of Central
Java, Yogyakarta, Central Kalimantan, and West
Kalimantan.
Poverty Category:             Program of Targeted Interventions:  the project would
target scholarships, contract teachers, new and alternative
schools, and at least 60 percent of quality interventions to
poor rural areas and girls
Amount:                       US$104.0 million equivalent
Terms:                        Repayable in 20 years including five years of grace at the
Bank's standard variable interest rate for currency pool
loans
Commitment Fee:               0.75 percent on undisbursed loan balances, beginning 60
days after signing, less any waiver
Financing Plan:               See Table 4.3
Economic Rate of Return:      The economic rate of return for the project investment
ranges from 15 to 18 percent by province. High returns
were found for both quality and access components.
Returns on quality enhancements were generally higher
than for expansion of access, particularly in the Kalimantan
provinces.

This report is based on the findings of an appraisal mission which visited Indonesia in April 1996,
comprising Donald Hamilton (mission leader), Sverrir Sigurdsson and Peter Morrison. The report also
drew on proposals prepared by the central and provincial MOEC and inputs from PHRD-funded consultants
(see Annex 13). Haneen Sayed assisted in the economic analysis of the report. Valuable comments were
provided by Brigitte Duces and Antonio Pereira. Cynthia Cristobal and Mercedes Bautista assisted in the
production of the report. Peer reviewers were Nisha Agrawal (EA3CO), Bruno Laporte (HDD), Ruth Kagia
(EAIHR), and Audrey Aarons (SAIPH). Marianne Haug, Director, EA3DR and Samuel Lieberman,
Acting Chief, EA3PH, have endorsed the report.

Staff Appraisal Report                             I
1. EDUCATION ISSUES AND STRATEGY
A.  BACKGROUND AND INTRODUCTION
1.1    Over the last 25 years, the Government of Indonesia has demonstrated a strong
commitment to education and as a result, Indonesia has achieved one of the best rates of
educational transition in the world. The Government-led enrollment drive and substantial school
building program in the 1970s sharply increased primary school enrollment. The gross enrollment
rate stood at 75 percent in the early 1970s but had increased to about 90 percent by the end of that
decade. By the mid-1980s, gross enrollment rates exceeded 100 percent and by 1994/95 had
reached 112 percent. The effort is not complete because of quality concerns and the need to
increase retention rates, but the achievements at the primary level have been substantial in this
diverse and far-flung archipelago. (Annexes 1-6 provide basic data on the educational structure
and administration in Indonesia.)
1.2   The Government of Indonesia (GOI) is looking to future needs, and has reiterated its
commitment to a proactive program in the future with expansion of junior secondary education as
a priority. While maintaining its commitment to primary education, the Government is aiming for
universal basic education (years 1-9) by 2008/09, to meet the human resource requirements for
transforming the economy from one based on natural resources to one based more on industry
and technology.
1.3   The decision to expand junior secondary education participation as rapidly as feasible is
justified for several reasons (see Annex 7). First, expansion appears to be a precondition for the
rapid growth of the Indonesian economy in the medium term.  Recent research on the
development of East Asian economies indicates that with the achievement of near universal
primary education, secondary (junior and senior) education has had a strong impact on economic
growth. Secondary education plays the role of providing educated entrants to the labor force,
enhancing the trainability of workers, and providing qualified inputs into higher levels of
education.
1.4   In this regard, the Indonesian economy and labor market are in the midst of a structural
transformation. Since 1990, there has been a marked slow-down in employment creation in
agriculture and an acceleration in the rate of growth of job creation in manufacturing,
construction, transport and communication. After a period of wage stagnation in the 1980s, there
are strong indications of rising real wage rates throughout the country and an absolute decline of
employment in agriculture. If sustained nationally for a decade or so, these trends imply the
absorption of remaining pockets of surplus labor, and as happened in other NICs, the
transformation from an agrarian economy to one relying on the education and productivity of its
workers. Social rates of return to education computed from 1986-92 indicate that returns to junior
secondary education are the highest among the education subsectors.



2                 Central Indonesia Junior Secondary Education Project
1.5    The second justification for significant Government attention to junior secondary
education is that the number of school places is still limited relative to demand, despite
substantial outlays by parents and a significant private sector response. This inadequacy is
evident from continued strong demand for generally more costly and often lower quality private
education, large numbers of unsuccessful applicants to schools, the prevalence of double shifts
and large classes in schools, and the use of libraries and laboratories for classroom teaching.
1.6    A third rationale for Government intervention is to assist in removing barriers of cost and
isolation that keep many of the rural poor out of school. In rural Java, for example, total expen-
diture per junior secondary student is equivalent to approximately 86 percent of per capita
household expenditure for households of the lowest quintile. Junior secondary education is
therefore beyond the reach of many of the poor and near poor. The opportunity costs of keeping
children in school exacerbate the problem. Expansion of junior secondary education in the
remote rural areas -- which will be the main areas of focus because of their very low enrollment
rates -- will be of direct benefit to the poor, especially girls who are sometimes disadvantaged in
these areas.
1.7    A fourth rationale relates to likely externalities associated with investments in secondary
education. Research from other countries has pointed to externalities such as reduced fertility and
child mortality and improved nutritional status that correlate with female secondary education.
For Indonesia, survey results indicate that a mother's education is an important determinant of
prenatal care utilization: more than 90 percent of women with junior secondary education use
prenatal care, in contrast to only 75 percent of women with some primary education.
Furthermore, analysis of nutritional status in Indonesia finds a strong effect of maternal education
on children's nutritional status: for women with between zero and five years of education, an
additional year of education has no impact on the child's nutritional status, while among women
with six to twelve years of education (or more), an additional year of education improves
nutritional status significantly.
1.8    The role of the Government would therefore be to (a) help expand the number of school
places through a combination of direct investment and measures to stimulate further private
sector/community participation in the provision of school places, and (b) ensure that the poor are
given increased opportunity to participate in the expansion process.
Sector Financing Plan
1.9    Achieving universal nine-year basic education will require significant additional public
resources to be directed to the education sector overall and to basic education in particular.
Estimates of the total costs of the junior secondary expansion program come to approximately
$7.6 billion to be phased out over a 15-year period (see Annex 7 for details). The cost estimates
assume that the private sector will maintain its share of total enrollment of about 40 percent.
During the next ten years of expansion, the budgetary shortfall is estimated at an average $120
million per annum. This is over and above additional resources generated by a 6 percent p.a.
growth of the budget. This implies that the budgetary allocation to junior secondary education
would need to increase from the current 13 percent of the education budget to approximately 16
percent in the next few years. Overall, Indonesia would need to increase its allocation to



Staff Appraisal Report                                  3
education as a proportion of GDP by approximately 0.2 percent (this does not take into account,
however, increases in other education subsectors, which may equally be required).
1.10   Government has demonstrated its commitment to expansion of junior secondary
education through increases in its education investments. In its most recent budget allocations
(1996/97) the education sector budget was increased by 20 percent, one of the highest increases
amongst all sectors. The budget for junior secondary education saw a 24 percent rise over last
year's budget allocation. GOI is also requesting help from donors to close the financing gap.
These efforts include proposed projects in junior secondary education in Indonesia for which
World Bank assistance will be sought over the next four to five fiscal years. The Central
Indonesia Junior Secondary Education Project covers four of the country's 27 provinces:
(a) Central Java (Jawa Tengah), (b) Central Kalimantan (Kalimantan Tengah), (c) West
Kalimantan (Kalimantan Barat), and (d) Yogyakarta (D.I. Yogyakarta). Other projects currently
under preparation are the East Java and East Nusa Tenggara Junior Secondary Education Project
and the Sumatra Junior Secondary Education Project.   The Secondary  School Teacher
Development Project (Loan 3979-IND) and the Book and Reading Development Project (Loan
3887-IND) will complement these efforts, as well as future basic and secondary education
operations in other provinces. To promote efficiency and equity in the use of resources, the
Government has recognized that educational development among and within the provinces must
be balanced and appropriate to local conditions, meet an acceptable standard of quality, and take
advantage of lessons learned within and outside the country.

1.11   Although the Government can and should promote expansion at the junior secondary
level, it is also aware that Indonesia will not be able to reach universal basic education enrollment
until graduation rates improve at the primary level. Despite high gross enrollment rates at the
primary level overall, about 25 percent of pupils, especially in the more difficult remote areas, do
not complete the full six years of education. Universal nine-year education thus depends on
improving the efficiency of the primary system and producing an expanded pool of candidates for
junior secondary school. The Government has started addressing this issue through a series of
initiatives, including the Primary Education Quality Improvement Project (Loan 3448-IND)
which is piloting measures for improving education quality. Satisfying existing unmet demand,
even at current primary-to-junior-secondary transition rates during the project period, is seen as
an important objective. This will help satisfy communities that are lukewarm to primary



4                 Central Indonesia Junior Secondary Education Project
education because of lack of opportunities for secondary education. The combined effects of
these measures would be to improve the transition rates and the potential for accelerating the
development of junior secondary education in the future.
Structure of the Education System
1.12   The education system in Indonesia consists of a six-year primary school cycle, junior and
senior secondary school cycles of three years each, and a postsecondary system ranging from one
to six years. Primary education is largely the domain of Government: nearly five out of six
pupils are enrolled in public schools. Private schooling plays a much greater role in secondary
education. Private schools account for 41 percent of enrollment in junior secondary education
and 54 percent in senior secondary. Junior secondary education is provided through 8,712 public
and 10,279 private general junior secondary schools (SMP) and a remaining 300 vocational
schools that emphasize either technical or home economics subjects, all of which are
administered by the Ministry of Education and Culture (MOEC). The Ministry of Religious
Affairs administers the 582 public and 7,547 private Muslim schools (Madrasahs, or MTs) at the
junior secondary level. At the senior secondary level, 3,241 public and 10,380 private schools
enroll a total of 4.2 million students, for a gross enrollment rate of 34 percent. Education statistics
are found in Annex 8.
1.13   When a private junior secondary school is established, it is classified as "listed" and is
evaluated after graduating its first students. Private schools can advance in accreditation from
"registered" to "recognized" and finally to the status of "equivalent" to public schools. Most
equivalent schools are owned and operated by religious organizations or large enterprises.
Registered and recognized schools are in general owned and operated by teachers' associations
and other nonprofit organizations (yayasans), although some are owned  by religious
organizations or small communities. Most listed schools are owned and operated by small rural
communities that do not have a public school nearby. Almost invariably, schools established by
communities aspire to complete Government takeover, while private yayasans often prefer partial
Government support so they can maintain control. Schools established by teachers' associations
often use public school facilities after hours and employ public school teachers as instructors.
B.    SECTOR ISSUES AND NATIONAL INITIATIVES
1.14   The project provinces face challenges in the provision of junior secondary education that
are common to all provinces in Indonesia, though not to the same extent. These challenges are
(a) to expand equitable access to junior secondary education; (b) to improve the quality of
education throughout the provinces; and (c) to make education management more efficient as the
provinces assume increased responsibility. Access is restricted mainly by the lack of places; high
direct, transportation, and opportunity costs; and great distances and limited transportation
networks in some provinces. In some instances parents in very remote or difficult areas do not
express interest in junior secondary education as they think it is beyond their reach. The most
important factors affecting quality and internal efficiency are: teacher knowledge and skills; the
availability of textbooks, teaching materials, and equipment; the curriculum; pedagogical



Staff Appraisal Report                               5
supervision; the flexibility of the school calendar; school management; and examinations and
educational assessment.
1.15   In spite of these constraints to access and quality, Indonesia has made great strides in
gender equality in both primary and junior secondary education. Only slightly fewer girls than
boys are enrolled in school at the national level, and the rates for three of the project provinces
are higher than for Indonesia as a whole, and in the case of Central Java equal to the national
average. Girls also have lower drop-out and repeater rates than boys and are often higher
achievers. However, rural conditions similar to those which present obstacles to the development
of junior secondary education also adversely affect the participation of girls in some
communities. When schools are located a long way from villages, parents are less willing to let
their daughters commute long distances for religious or cultural reasons, or undertake the costly
expenditures of a long commute or relocation to urban areas. Consequently, boys in these
communities get to go to school rather than girls or go to the better schools often located away
from the remote villages. This happens particularly in certain remote areas of the Kalimantan
provinces.
1.16  In addition to access and quality, all provinces face management challenges as a result of
the deconcentration of tasks from the center to the provinces, a process this project will support.
For historical and political reasons, public administration in Indonesia has been highly centralized
and decision making is tightly controlled by central Government in Jakarta. The center has
traditionally set objectives for education expansion and development, with few inputs from the
provinces. However, the differences of local conditions in each province and district, accentuated
by the great distances of the outer islands from the administrative center, make it increasingly
difficult for central administration to devise and implement satisfactory solutions to local
problems. Although Jakarta will retain a significant role in providing policy guidelines and
promoting regional equity and overall economic development, the Government has decided
gradually to devolve authority to the provinces and districts. This devolution will result in a
deconcentration of the functions of the MOEC to provincial (Kanwil) and district (Kandep)
education offices.
Initiatives Related to Access
1.17   GOI is aware of these issues and a number of initiatives have been put in place to address
them. To improve access, the policy of establishing a minimum size of nine classrooms for
Government schools, which has prevented opening schools in small communities, is being
changed to make it easier for schools with fewer classrooms to be built. Most rural areas also find
it difficult to attract teachers, so the Government is planning to hire contract teachers for limited
terms.
1.18   To make up for some of the inadequacies of the public sector, private schools supported
by local communities, private educational foundations and religious groups have been established
and account for about 40 percent of total enrollment at the junior secondary level. GOI supports
these schools through a variety of measures, including provision of teachers, but compared to
public schools, they are generally of lower quality and cost more to parents. The low quality in
these schools is explained by poor quality teachers and even greater scarcity of educational



6                 Central Indonesia Junior Secondary Education Project
materials than in public schools. Unit costs of operations in these schools is, however, lower than
in public schools. Current and proposed GOI support for private education would help maintain
or increase its share of enrollment and improve quality.
1.19  In some sparsely populated areas some communities are not even large enough to support
smaller schools; students have to travel over long distances or difficult terrain to get to school, so
other alternatives are being implemented. The SMP Terbuka ("open SMP") program has been
established to meet the needs of students who cannot attend regular schools because of distance
or high direct and opportunity costs. These are non-fee-paying schools. and students attend
afternoon classes run by secondary school teachers one or more times a week at a center, usually
a junior secondary school, supplemented by tutorials in their villages. The effectiveness of these
schools have been in question because of scarcity of learning materials and inadequate
operational funds. As a result, student drop-out has been a serious problem in some areas.
1.20   To assist rural families who cannot afford to pay for education, GOI has established a
scholarship program for children of low economic background and satisfactory academic
achievement and personality.  This program  covers all costs related to junior secondary
education; within the eligible group priority is given to children from remote areas, orphans, and
girls. GOI plans to expand this program to improve its effectiveness. See Annex 10 for options
to increase access to junior secondary education.
Initiatives Related to Quality
1.21   Various initiatives have also been introduced to improve the quality of junior secondary
education, including efforts to bring good teachers to underserved areas. Although there is an
excess number of teachers in the aggregate, there is an unequal distribution of teachers between
urban and rural areas within provinces because most rural teaching positions are considered
undesirable. Soon after being appointed to rural areas, many teachers try to be placed into urban
schools or find excuses to be absent for long periods. To address this issue of shortages of
(particularly core subject) teachers in rural and remote areas, some provinces have experimented
with hiring "contract teachers" in rural schools in core academic areas. Some rural communities
have also provided their teachers with dormitories. In addition, the Government is already
providing some financial compensation for service in hardship areas. Over the longer term, local
recruitment is one practice that will help to keep teachers in rural areas.
1.22   The unequal distribution of teachers has rather different consequences in the urban areas
where many teachers are underutilized, and where there is a perception of high levels of
absenteeism. Financial rewards may not be the only answer. Teachers generally spend only a
limited time in school because of the very few hours they are assigned to teach, the lack of
teacher rooms, inadequate supplies of library, reference and teaching materials, and the absence
of a structure which promotes interaction among teachers in schools. Management is therefore
focused on purely administrative issues rather than the educational process. And the opportunity
to promote improved teaching in the various subjects is lost. Commitment to outside activities is
therefore sometimes greater than to teaching. Teachers recognize this gap and have effectively
organized monthly subject teacher meetings as a part of their associations (MGMP) outside the
school system. Similar groups are to be encouraged in schools, to help motivate teachers. These



Staff Appraisal Report                               7
groups would gradually be institutionalized as subject departments within which teachers would
take collective responsibility for effective teaching of a given subject. This initiative, combined
with efforts to reduce the over-supply of teachers and to provide teacher rooms and better
libraries in schools, should increase teacher effectiveness.
1.23   Developed from an initiative taken by the MGMP teacher associations, an inservice
teacher training program called PKG was introduced in 1983 to improve the subject-matter
knowledge and skills of teachers (see Annex 11 for details). This program was highly successful
in improving the pedagogical skills of teachers in the selected subject areas and created a positive
attitude among teachers where it was provided. There were, however, some serious shortcomings
of the program. The training curriculum did not fully address the subject-matter knowledge of
teachers, development of skills in identifying learning difficulties was neglected, and teachers in
rural areas could not be covered because of high costs and logistical difficulties. Training was
provided every weekend followed by classroom visits, and trainees were supposed to develop
their own materials. Some of the rural teachers who could be reached could not take full
advantage of the program because of weak qualifications. The decentralization of the PKG and
MGMP system to the district level and the systematic involvement of regional MOEC staff,
including supervisors and principals in the training program, would help address this issue.
1.24   Providing incentives to teachers to spend more time on teaching and reduce absenteeism
also needs to be considered. The costs of this can be high, however, as a general salary increase
for all educational staff would have large fiscal implications on the overall civil service wage bill.
Hence, specific teaching hour supplements that are phased in and linked to performance may be
introduced if an efficient management system for this can be developed.
1.25   Efforts to provide schools with science equipment have not been completely successful.
Procurement and distribution of equipment from Jakarta has resulted in difficult distribution
problems and the supply of inappropriate equipment to schools. Changes in the provision of
science equipment and other school materials are being piloted in the ongoing Second Secondary
Education and Management Project (Loan 3158-IND), including requiring suppliers to distribute
equipment directly to schools as part of their contracts.
1.26   The public examinations and testing system also has been improved under the Second
Secondary Education and Management Project, but the process still needs to be completed. While
preparation of test items for multiple-choice examinations have been greatly improved,
development of open-ended and essay type questions still need to be addressed, and adequate
mechanisms for objective scoring and analysis of results are only now being put in place.
Through the same project, a national assessment system is being established to monitor the
performance of the system regularly. Parallel with the development of the public system the
quality of tests and identification of learning weaknesses in schools has also been initiated so that
educational problems in individual schools can be addressed. A combination of the development
of the public and private systems would provide teachers and managers with the required
information for planning development of the system.



8                  Central Indonesia Junior Secondary Education Project
C.  JUNIOR SECONDARY EDUCATION IN THE PROVINCES
1.27   The issues constraining further development of junior secondary education affect the four
project provinces in significantly different ways, and they have developed their education
strategies in response to local circumstances, needs, and opportunities. Central Java's developing
economy can absorb a high rate of growth in junior secondary education but is constrained by
rural conditions and a rate of poverty that is higher than the national average. The two
Kalimantan provinces are held back by geography and poverty, but the need for expansion is less
urgent, particularly in Central Kalimantan where opportunities for junior secondary school
graduates are fewer and the pool of primary graduates is limited. Yogyakarta has already made
substantial progress in expanding access to junior secondary education, so it is focusing its efforts
on traditional and new measures to improve quality. Private schools have been remarkably
successful in providing places for over 40 percent of students in three of the provinces, but at
tremendous cost to parents. While Central Java has a relatively robust network of private schools
in some areas, most such schools in rural Kalimantan are struggling to survive among poor,
scattered villages, where the public sector's help is more crucial. In Central Kalimantan the role
of the private sector is restricted (30 percent of enrollments) because conditions are less
conducive for its development. Because of the diverse conditions in the project provinces, a
variety of instruments would be used as appropriate to increase access and improve the quality of
secondary education.

Central Java
1.28   Socioeconomic Background. Central Java is densely populated, rural, and poor, but is
changing rapidly. Although small in area, Central Java is the third largest province in Indonesia
with about 30 million inhabitants. The population aged 13-15 peaked in 1993 at just over 2
million and is projected to continue declining throughout the project period. About 70 percent of
the population lives in rural areas, compared to 78 percent in 1980. Central Java has 20 percent of
the country's population living in poverty: over 7 million people had income levels below the



Staff Appraisal Report                               9
poverty line in 1990. The province is below the average for Indonesia in household income and
per capita expenditure, and is the second lowest of the four project provinces. However, per
capita GDP growth (6 percent) was higher than the Indonesian average (4.8 percent) from 1983-
1993.
1.29   The province's employed labor force is still concentrated in agriculture (40 percent in
1994), although the importance of the sector has fallen sharply (from 48 percent in 1987) as
people shift into urban occupations. Urbanization and continued GDP growth have created a
demand for increasing numbers of educated workers -- this is reflected in the wage premiums
enjoyed by more educated wage earners. In 1992, employees with only primary education
received an average monthly wage of about Rp 69,000, those with junior secondary education
earned 27 percent more, those with senior secondary education earned 56 percent more than
junior secondary education graduates, and those with tertiary education earned 52 percent more
than senior secondary graduates.
1.30   Education System. Central Java enrolls 4.5 million primary school pupils in about
26,000 schools and accounts for 15 percent of the nation's primary enrollment. The province's
gross enrollment rate is 114 percent at the primary level, compared to a junior secondary rate of
62 percent. The system enrolls 1.3 million junior secondary school students, compared to
340,000 in the other three provinces combined. As in all the project provinces, private schooling
is crucial in helping to fill the gap between demand and supply. About 42 percent of the
province's students attend private junior secondary schools, and there are twice as many private
schools as public schools. Most of these private schools are small (about a third the size of the
average public school) but relatively well-established, 80 percent having reached the
"recognized" or "equivalent" level of accreditation. The private sector plays an even greater role
at the senior secondary level, where it accounts for 60 percent of the total enrollment of 600,000
and 72 percent of the schools. The senior secondary gross enrollment rate in Central Java is 32
percent, slightly lower than the national rate.
1.31   Subsector Issues. The scale of Central Java's education system is enormous and presents
the greatest challenges, in terms of absolute numbers, of any of the project provinces. Access to
junior secondary education is highly variable throughout the province, based on income levels,
and urban or rural living. Gross enrollment rates range from 42 percent to 109 percent by district.
The rural enrollment rate is 46 percent compared to an urban rate of 77 percent. Of the 35
districts, eight account for half of the province's 13-15 age population not attending school. The
provincial enrollment rate improved from 1992/93 to 1994/95 as junior secondary enrollment
expanded even as the cohort population began to level off. Still, large numbers are not in school.
A total of 775,000 youth of the appropriate age are not attending junior secondary school, and
each year more than 160,000 pupils graduate from primary school but do not continue their
studies.
1.32   Access is restricted in Central Java, particularly in rural areas, mainly because capacity is
limited and because direct and opportunity costs are high. Public and private schools have
expanded their enrollment rapidly to meet demand for school places, especially in cities and in
other areas that are easy to reach. For example, private school enrollment increased by 26
percent over two years, 1992/93-1994/95. The number of students per classroom averages 46, so



10                Central Indonesia Junior Secondary Education Project
specialized teaching classrooms such as laboratories and libraries often get used as regular
classrooms to house the overflow of students. In addition, teachers favor urban schools while
rural schools often do not have enough qualified staff. In rural low-income areas, many parents
are hindered by high costs, even when places are available. Aside from the opportunity costs of
sending children to school during working hours, rural households devote an exceptionally high
share of their expenditure to junior secondary education, as noted earlier. Even in those rural
areas where public transportation is available, the cost of a two-way trip every day contributes to
these expenses and is probably prohibitive for low-income families.
1.33   The quality of education is also a concern, particularly in rural schools. Low quality
reduces the interest of students and parents and leads to inefficiencies in the system as students
score poorly on examinations, drop out, or repeat grades. Quality is particularly affected by the
skills of teachers and the availability of instructional tools and facilities. Teachers need ongoing
support and training because many are teaching with a weak educational background. Two-thirds
of the province's SMP teachers do not have the required level of qualification, and 12 percent
have no more than a secondary education (this rises to one third among Madrasah teachers). Only
one teacher in five has received ongoing training and support under the current inservice program
(PKG). Private schools tend to rely heavily on less-qualified part-time teachers. Whereas only 14
percent of the public school teachers are part-time, the ratio is 55 percent in private schools.
1.34   In addition, the facilities and teaching tools that teachers have to work with are limited.
Among SMPs, which are generally better equipped than Madrasahs, 68 percent have little or no
science teaching equipment. A third of the schools have no library, and many that do have few
books. On average, school libraries have about two books per student; many of those are extra
copies of textbooks and teacher handbooks, or are out of date and of poor quality. Textbooks for
students have historically been limited, though this problem is being addressed through another
Bank project. Because of the province's poverty, limited parental contributions also affect
quality. Of family expenditure on junior secondary education, only 6 percent goes for textbooks,
compared to a national average of 13 percent.
1.35   Broad Subsector Strategy. To respond to the likely rise in demand for junior secondary
education in light of continuing industrial and urban development, GOI plans to aggressively
support equitable expansion of junior secondary education in Central Java, although near-
universal access cannot be achieved until a larger proportion of primary school pupils graduate
from that level. Several types of support are planned. First, the province will build schools in
and attract good teachers to areas of low enrollment, particularly larger rural settlements that the
private sector is not covering. Second, it will further support the strong tradition of private
schooling in this province by promoting quality improvement and more efficient administration,
so parents will see it as providing education of a standard similar to that of public schools. Third,
the province will alleviate financial constraints that keep the rural poor out of school, in part by
reducing travel costs and by providing flexible education alternatives that do not force young
people to choose between study and work. Fourth, improvement of the overall quality of
education, particularly in rural areas, will increase the trainability of junior secondary school
graduates from both public and private schools. Because of its large size and dense population,
Central Java's strategies for implementing quality improvements would be different from those of
the other provinces. For example, the inservice teacher training program strategy in the province



Staff Appraisal Report                              11
would focus on decentralization to the district level, and greater support would be given to
MGMPs.
Yogyakarta
1.36   Socioeconomic Background. Yogyakarta, small in land area, is the most heavily
urbanized province in Indonesia, aside from Jakarta. Between 1980 and 1990, the urban
population grew at a rate of 8 percent per annum. Still, over 40 percent of the people live in rural
areas. Rapid urbanization has produced the lowest share of the labor force in agriculture (45
percent) among the four project provinces and the highest share in services (37 percent).
Furthermore, social development indicators such as school attendance are among the highest in
Indonesia. Yet GDP per capita is the lowest among the project provinces. High enrollment in
junior secondary school is reflected in reduced wage premiums for educated workers. In 1992,
the wage premium for workers with junior secondary education over those with primary
education was only 11 percent, but that for senior secondary graduates over those with junior
secondary education was 52 percent.
1.37   Education System.  Yogyakarta has been remarkably successful in expanding the
coverage of education. Of youth aged 10-19 years, 82 percent attend school and only 12 percent
are working. Yogyakarta's primary school system has 355,000 pupils and a gross enrollment rate
of 118 percent. About 80 percent of the primary schools are public, compared to 39 percent at
the junior secondary level. A majority of junior secondary students, however, attend public
schools rather than the smaller private schools. Yogyakarta and Jakarta are the two provinces that
are farthest ahead of the national average in coverage at the lower secondary level. Yogyakarta's
junior secondary system as a whole has a high gross enrollment rate of 89 percent. Most students
attend general SMPs: out of a total of 547 junior secondary schools, the number of vocational
schools in the province has dwindled to only three. Also, the role of Madrasahs, which are
generally of lower quality than SMPs, is less significant here than in the other project provinces
(they account for only 3 percent of all students). At the senior secondary level, the gross
enrollment rate is an impressive 62 percent, compared to the national rate of 34 percent. The
private system accounts for 76 percent of the senior secondary schools and 60 percent of the
students. Yogyakarta has a strong tradition of education as reflected in its high gross enrollment
rates even at the tertiary level - the highest in the country and more than three times the national
average.
1.38   Subsector Issues. As a result of Yogyakarta's high population density, access to junior
secondary schools is far less of a problem here than in the other project provinces. In Bantul and
Gunung Kidul districts, which are rural, enrollment rates are lower, partly because of school costs
and partly because some young people go to work or to school in the city. These districts account
for two-thirds of the province's out-of-school youth in the 13-15 age group. The relative quality
of the system is reflected in national examination scores, which are among the highest in the
country. Interest in education is high, and the secondary school system has been supported
through links with the tertiary institutions. The province as a whole, therefore, is aiming for
excellence, although parts of Yogyakarta are still limited by weaknesses in teaching and by
limited facilities and instructional tools. In general, Yogyakarta's private schools are relatively



12               Central Indonesia Junior Secondary Education Project
well developed, particularly in comparison to those in the Kalimantan provinces. Over 90
percent have reached the accreditation level of "recognized" or "equivalent".
1.39   Broad Subsector Strategy. By continuing to target remaining pockets with lower school
attendance, Yogyakarta can be expected to achieve near-universal junior secondary education
from the current 89 percent within the next few years, without donor assistance. The province
now concentrates on improving the quality of both junior and senior secondary education, which
are already producing many well-performing graduates. The public and private school systems do
this by setting and monitoring high standards, creating centers of excellence, trying new
technologies, and training staff. Because of its achievements and small size, Yogyakarta is
beginning to serve as a national model for testing the next generation of approaches in classroom
instruction. As it does so, the province will develop mechanisms to evaluate the success of its
innovations, share lessons learned with other provinces, and expand its participation in
international comparisons of educational achievement. Yogyakarta will continue to support its
private schools through training and the provision of other quality inputs.
Central Kalimantan
1.40   Socioeconomic Background. Central Kalimantan has the smallest and most scattered
population of the four provinces. Although it remains sparsely inhabited, the population is
growing and becoming more urbanized. Four out of five people live in rural areas, but the urban
population grew by an average of 10 percent a year from 1980-1990. Nine rivers provide the
main transportation links between towns and villages. About 15 percent of the population lives
in isolated villages of 100-200 people in remote mountainous and border areas, where travel is
often extremely difficult. The indigenous Dayak groups of Central Kalimantan have traditionally
practiced subsistence farming using slash-and-bum techniques. Transmigration largely from Java
has brought plantation development and other changes to some rural areas. The Government has
proposed to expand this program in the coming years, particularly in Kotawaringin Timur district.
About 60 percent of the province's villages are classified by the Government as poor villages.
1.41   Employment in Central Kalimantan is concentrated in agriculture (65 percent) while
manufacturing absorbs only 7 percent of the labor force. The province is rich in natural resources
which are being exploited in enclave fashion. For this reason, GDP per capita is the highest
among the four project provinces, though it grew more slowly (3 percent) than the national
average during 1983-1993 and was not broadly distributed. Wage premiums for more highly
educated workers are narrower than the average for Indonesia. The current structure of the
economy has not begun to marginalize those workers with low educational qualification.
1.42   Education System. Primary schools in Central Kalimantan enroll 265,000 students for a
gross enrollment rate of 108 percent, although many do not finish the full six years. At the junior
secondary level, the gross enrollment rate is 50 percent, compared to the national rate of 58
percent. The system enrolls just 59,000 junior secondary school students (compared to over a
million in Central Java), the smallest number of the four provinces. About 80 percent of those of
junior secondary school age live in rural areas. The two districts of Kotawaringin Timur and
Kapuas account for nearly three-quarters of the province's out-of-school youth of junior
secondary school age. Public schools enroll about twice as many students as private schools not



StaffAppraisal Report                               13
only at the junior secondary level but also at the senior secondary level (unlike the Java
provinces, where private schooling predominates at the senior level). The senior secondary gross
enrollment rate (30 percent) is less than half Yogyakarta's rate.
1.43   Subsector Issues. Low population density in both Kalimantan provinces (for example,
11 persons per square kilometer in Central Kalimantan compared to over 900 in Yogyakarta)
compounds the difficulty of providing access to secondary education, although the absolute
number of out-of-school youth is relatively low. In addition, primary school retention and
graduation rates are particularly low in this province, a weakness that limits the potential for
junior secondary expansion. Many rural areas of Central Kalimantan are not easily reached, have
populations that are too small for a regular SMP, and are in poor districts. As a result, the
province's urban gross enrollment rate at the junior secondary level is nearly double the rural
gross enrollment rate. This disparity is significantly more pronounced in both Kalimantan
provinces than in the nation as a whole. Because the population is small and scattered, the
average size of junior secondary schools here (I70) is less than half the average enrollment in
high-density Central Java (357). Private schools have an average of only about 100 students each.
Distance and the lack of accommodations prevent students in remote areas from attending school.
1.44   Costs also represent an access barrier, though many parents are willing to make
significant sacrifices to send children to school. Despite the fact that household expenses at
private schools are higher than in public schools, the number of private schools has increased
over the past decade, though they enroll a smaller proportion of students here than in the other
provinces. It is difficult to make private schools viable in many parts of Central Kalimantan
because of the undeveloped formal economy and difficult terrain, and unlike West Kalimantan,
there are fewer religious and other organizations involved. As a result, about 63 percent of
private schools are relatively underdeveloped (in the lowest two categories of the private school
classification). In addition to direct costs, opportunity costs are also high for students' families
who rely on teenagers for agriculture, fishing, and household chores during normal school hours.
Pockets of economic activity (such as gold mining) offer young people with less than a secondary
school education some opportunities for wage employment.
1.45   The quality of schooling also suffers in the isolated, poor communities of the two
Kalimantan provinces. In 1994/95, the average national exam scores in all the province's schools
were moderate to poor; none was classified as good. Twenty percent of teachers in Central
Kalimantan have only a secondary school education themselves. Isolation often cuts teachers off
from  further training or peer support.  These teachers need ongoing training, upgrading,
monitoring, and mentoring to strengthen their subject matter knowledge, pedagogical skills, and
confidence in the classroom. But many of the province's teachers have not been covered by the
PKG training program because of their isolation. In addition, the highest concentration of the
teaching force is in urban areas where living conditions are better and adequate housing is
available, while rural areas have an acute undersupply of qualified subject teachers. Due to the
shortage of teachers, small rural junior secondary schools generally have multisubject teachers
who are often not trained adequately in all subjects they teach, if in any.
1.46   In rural areas low quality of teaching is compounded by the widespread employment of
part-time teachers, often poorly qualified and motivated. Increasing the challenge of teaching is



14               Central Indonesia Junior Secondary Education Project
the shortage of appropriate teaching materials and facilities. Science is most often taught by
lecture in regular classrooms with no teaching aids or equipment. Parents use only 3 percent of
junior secondary schooling expenditure for textbooks.
1.47   Broad Subsector Strategy. Central Kalimantan will continue to expand junior secondary
education, but the scale of the expansion will be relatively modest given the limited wage
differentials between primary and secondary graduates and the small number and proportion of
students completing the primary system. Because community-supported private schools are
particularly weak in this province, the planned role of the public sector is crucial. First, Central
Kalimantan will direct its investment at geographically isolated populations with low enrollment
rates, where the province's limited private school system is not functioning. In these areas, the
province will employ a range of options to reach potential students, such as building small
schools to serve small communities, attracting teachers to underserved areas, supporting village-
based schooling, and providing accommodation for students. Second, quality will be improved to
increase the value of a junior secondary education, a development that would encourage students
to complete the primary cycle and would help prepare students for further training as the
province's natural resources are increasingly exploited. While maintaining quality in its own
schools, the province will also support the relatively weak private sector so it can remain viable,
particularly in remote areas. Third, the province will design its interventions to ease the financial
burden of the poor.
West Kalimantan
1.48   Socioeconomic Background. About a third of West Kalimantan's population was
considered to be living in poverty in 1990, a high rate for Indonesia. However, the province is
undergoing rapid economic and demographic change: GDP per capita grew by 6 percent
annually 1983-1993, one of the highest rates in the country. The primary port city of Pontianak is
a growing center for industry and regional trade. Traditionally, the province has relied on river
transport, like Central Kalimantan. However, roads are becoming more important for the 79
percent of the population living in rural areas and have expanded the province's trade with
bordering Sarawak (Malaysian Borneo). As a result of this growth, isolated families and entire
villages are shifting from river areas to road areas to gain access to goods, services, and jobs. In
addition, because of the province's low population density, GOI is resettling volunteer
transmigrants from other parts of Indonesia in rural plantation areas (especially rubber and palm
oil), where the soil is not fertile enough for growing rice.
1.49   West Kalimantan's economy remains heavily dependent on agriculture, and 68 percent of
employed people were in that sector in 1993. But the nature of the agricultural activities (for
example, the plantation crops) has put per capita incomes close to the average for Indonesia. In
their turn, satisfactory average per capita income and higher economic growth rates have resulted
in higher than average salaries for employed persons of all levels of education attainment, but
especially at primary and junior secondary levels. A corollary of this is that wage premiums for
educated workers are smaller than in other provinces (16 percent for junior secondary graduates
and 5 percent for senior secondary graduates). However, many people in rural areas are largely
outside the formal economy.



StaffAppraisal Report                               15
1.50   Education System. At the primary level, West Kalimantan has about 620,000 pupils in
3,870 schools, 90 percent of which are public. The primary gross enrollment rate is 111 percent.
The secondary school enrollment rates are the lowest of the four project provinces. At the junior
secondary level, the gross enrollment rate is 46 percent, compared to the national rate of 58
percent. The junior secondary system enrolls 122,000 students, about 51,000 of them in private
schools. The proportion of schools that are public or private Madrasahs has doubled over the past
decade. Schools in plantation areas adjacent to indigenous Dayak communities sometimes serve a
mixture of ethnic and religious groups. In fact, unlike the other project provinces, where most
SMP students are Islamic, SMP students in West Kalimantan are as likely to be either Christian
or Buddhist as Muslim. At the senior secondary level, the gross enrollment ratio is 24 percent
(compared to the national rate of 34 percent). Although public schools account for just over a
quarter of the total number of senior secondary schools in the province, they enroll as many
students as private schools.
1.51   Subsector Issues. As in other parts of Kalimantan, access to education is restricted by
distance and cost. As a result, the rural enrollment rate in West Kalimantan is less than a third of
the enrollment rate in urban areas; in contrast, nationally the rural rate is more than half the urban
rate. Although the most populated districts near the coastal road and river networks account for
the majority of the province's youth who are not in junior secondary school, the more remote and
sparsely populated districts (such as Sintang, Sanggau, and Ketapang) have lower enrollment
rates. Much of the population lives in small villages scattered over difficult terrain that have an
insufficient number of young people to justify a separate school of typical size. Transportation to
the nearest school is often a great distance by foot or by river, and flooding during the rainy
season isolates villages from each other. Schools in such areas also find it difficult to attract good
teachers because of harsh local conditions.
1.52   If they have a school at all, remote villages are often served by a small private school.
The average size of junior secondary schools in the province as a whole (191) is smaller here than
in the Java provinces. A large number of rural private schools have been established by
communities, with limited resources, as a last resort. However, many of them are weak: 80
percent of all private schools have not advanced in their accreditation beyond the basic
"registered" status. In some villages, Madrasahs open where the public sector has not reached.
They are more acceptable than public schools to many Muslim parents of females, partly because
of their religious instruction and partly because of their proximity to the village. However, the
costs of private Madrasahs and other schools is more than many can afford. In addition to high
direct costs relative to rural incomes, opportunity costs in certain areas limit enrollment. Young
people in certain subdistricts can find forestry jobs or work in home industries, and many are
needed to help their parents at home or in economic activities such as fishing or farming during
morning school hours.
1.53   The qusality of the small, private schools that are common in remote areas is often low.
National examination (NEM) scores for the province declined between 1992/93 and 1994/95 and
were especially weak in science, mathematics, and English. Private school teachers are generally
less qualified than their public school counterparts: more than half have no more than a senior
secondary school education. Of all teachers, in both public and private schools, only 11 percent
have the newly required qualification of DIII degree, and about 30 percent even have no training



16               Central Indonesia Junior Secondary Education Project
beyond secondary school. Because of their isolation, many receive limited or no inservice
training or onservice support. The relatively high costs met by families affect not only access but
quality. After paying the necessary registration fees and other costs needed to establish a school
or enroll their children, parents and school associations often cannot afford significant amounts to
ensure students get a quality education. For example, families spend more than three times as
much for school uniforms as for textbooks.
1.54   Broad Subsector Strategy.  Junior secondary education in this province will be
developed at a moderate pace to help the province manage and take advantage of demographic
and economic change. The province will give special attention to expanding education for the
majority of the rural population who are unlikely to benefit substantially from rapid change
unless they are better educated. This agenda will be supported in several ways. First, while
addressing barriers of geography and distance by placing small schools in remote areas, the
province will also take advantage of village consolidation and improved transportation networks
by placing somewhat larger schools in more densely populated areas. Public schools will be
placed in such rural settlements where private schools have not developed, either because of
isolation or low regional incomes. Stronger private schools, including religious schools, will be
allowed to grow without competition from the public sector. Second, quality will be addressed
urgently, particularly in rural schools that have difficulty training unqualified teachers and
keeping good ones. Relatively weak community-supported schools, in particular, will be given
special consideration. Third, the province's expansion efforts will be designed to keep the cost of
education reasonable for the high number of people living in poverty.



Staff Appraisal Report                            17
2. GOVERNMENT AND BANK STRATEGY
AND LESSONS LEARNED
A.  GOVERNMENT STRATEGY
2.1    As part of the Repelita VI (1994/99) program, the Government set the important goal of
extending access to high quality junior secondary education. This objective is integral to the goal
of attaining universal coverage of nine years of basic education by the end of Repelita VIII
(2008/09). To support this ambitious goal, the Government has adopted several policy measures
and strategies (see Annex 9): (a) it plans to provide additional resources to basic education,
starting with an increase of 20 percent in the overall budget of education for the 1996/97 fiscal
year; (b) it has abolished junior secondary school fees and admission requirements for primary
school graduates; (c) it is providing free textbooks for all primary and junior secondary
schoolchildren; (d) it is supporting private junior secondary schools with public teachers and
other inputs to maintain and even increase their significant enrollment participation at this school
level; (e) it continues to upgrade teachers' skills for both public and private schools through
inservice programs; (f) it has started the construction or development of regular or alternative
schools in rural and remote villages; (g) it has phased out all diploma courses for the preservice
education of secondary school teachers (junior and senior) so all new teachers at that level now
need to have an SI (undergraduate degree); (h) it is upgrading existing junior secondary school
teachers to DII (two-year post-secondary diploma) at public expense; and (i) it is extending
subsidies (scholarships) to capable but poor students to begin or continue junior secondary
education.
B.    LESSONS LEARNED
2.2   Integration of the following lessons from previous Bank projects would help build on the
exceptional performance of the Bank in the education sector in Indonesia. All the 21 previous
projects have been judged satisfactory by OED, and they have set the stage for the more
challenging tasks in this sector. OED Document No. 9752, Indonesian Education and the World
Bank: an Assessment of Two Decades of Lending (1991) found that support for education
projects was timely and that the Bank gave valuable advice to the Government. Although the
projects were successful, there is a continuing need to improve the quality of education and to
strengthen management at the provincial level, particularly in light of the current trend of
deconcentrating responsibilities, a process this project will support.
2.3    OED Report. The 1991 report has several conclusions and recommendations that are
relevant for this project:
Issue: Institution building is basic to the long-term goal of increasing the self-sufficiency
of the Indonesian education system (p. 58).



18               Central Indonesia Junior Secondary Education Project
Project Response: Increased emphasis on institution building and self-management at
provincial and district levels.
Issue: Projects must be placed and discussed within the framework of the policy dialogue
(p. 65).
Project Response: Regular discussions between MOEC and the Bank on development
priorities and the role of the Bank related to other donors are instrumental in determining
agenda for Bank lending.
Issue: Despite explicit goals, projects have not always achieved equitable distribution of
project benefits to the poor (p. 68).
Project Response: Several project interventions are specifically targeted to the poor:
school construction in rural areas, SMP Terbuka, and the scholarship program.
Issue: Previous projects (mainly national in scope) were often overly complex or
underestimated absorption constraints and experience, resulting in unsatisfactory
implementation (p. 70).
Project Response: The project has been designed with extensive participation from
communities, existing schools, district and provincial MOEC staff.
Issue: GOI should spend a greater amount of its budget on education, to achieve the
necessary quality and institutional objectives (p. 78).
Project Response: Indonesia's education expenditures are 14 percent of the total
Government budget; the 1996/97 budget shows an increase of 20 percent over last year,
of which 72 percent is spent on basic education (primary and junior secondary).
Issue: Despite its role in providing educational opportunities, the private system has
received limited support from GOI and the Bank (p. 79).
Project Response: GOI is committed to ensuring that the contribution from the private
sector in junior secondary education is maintained and strengthened; recent projects have
provided private schools with quality inputs.
2.4    Lessons from  Completed and Ongoing Projects in Secondary Education. Other
lessons have been drawn or confirmed by the General Operational Review of Secondary
Education Projects by HDD. Implementation of the Indonesia Secondary Education Project
(Loan 2472-IND) and the ongoing Second Secondary Education and Management Project (Loan
3158-fND) are particularly relevant in this respect.
(a)    Overall Project Design.  The expansion of secondary education should be
addressed through a dual strategy of increasing school places and improving the
quality of education. The proposed project would include both expansion and
quality improvement objectives, to ensure continual demand for education and to
improve repetition and drop-out rates.



Staff Appraisal Report                            19
(b)   Flexibility in the Design of Components.  Attempts to impose standard
solutions to the variety of conditions in Indonesia (as in all large countries) have
not been successful, and GOI is working to correct this situation. Among others,
the policy of limiting the size of government schools to a minimum of nine
classrooms has been reviewed, to allow Government schools to be established in
communities with small populations and experimentation has started with
alternative models of junior secondary education.
(c)    Ownership and Participation. Local communities contribute significantly to
the development of education in their areas. Stakeholders and clients are also
well placed to evaluate project outcomes. They know when children are learning
and how investments in schools are being used. Stakeholder involvement (which
was emphasized during the preparation process, see Annex 13) is critical to fully
measure project outcomes, to understand how a project is unfolding and to help
develop appropriate corrective measures when necessary.  The project will
promote and supplement the use of local resources for education by providing
assistance in kind and incentives for both private and public education.
C.  BANK STRATEGY AND RATIONALE FOR INVOLVEMENT
2.5    This project -- one of the first in a series of junior secondary education projects --
supports the Bank's 1995 Country Assistance Strategy (CAS) for Indonesia, which was
presented to the Board on March 21, 1995. The CAS endorses Government's role towards
establishing a regulatory framework and in supporting decentralization and participation. In
addition, the CAS highlights the importance of continued emphasis on human resource
development to promote economic growth, competitiveness, and structural transformation, and
on a more selective, efficient use of public resources. The design of the proposed project is also
consistent with the Bank's strategy of balanced assistance to the education sector, which includes
on-going and proposed assistance for (a) improving the quality of primary education, including
possible interventions in early childhood development, (b) expansion and quality improvement of
lower-secondary education, (c) quality improvement of upper-secondary education, (d) selective
interventions in higher education, and (e) an active policy dialogue designed to redefine and
refocus the Government's role in the education sector.
2.6   The Bank's comparative advantage in supporting junior secondary education is based on
its long-standing and close working relationship with MOEC. The Bank has engaged in a
comprehensive dialogue with MOEC that reaches far beyond this project itself, since it has
helped: (a) establish a solid conceptual and economic framework for expanding and improving
the quality of the basic education in Indonesia over the next 20 years; (b) identify the comparative
advantages for Bank's and other donors' role in the planned expansion and quality improvement
process; and (c) strengthen the decentralizing trends toward sub-central levels, province-specific
projects, and local ownership. As a result of this dialogue, the previous highly centralized,
uniform approach to project design and implementation has been replaced by an approach that
will rest on provincially based projects. The Government has now recognized the need to take
into account specific provincial situations, including economic growth trends and prospects, the



20               Central Indonesia Junior Secondary Education Project
existing education needs, labor market conditions, private sector delivery capacity, levels of
poverty, and geographic conditions. The Bank has also been an active partner in the discussions
on the priorities governing basic education delivery, on the role of the private sector in secondary
education and on the share of Government spending that should be allocated to basic education.
2.7   The Bank is a trusted partner in basic education in Indonesia and has substantial
experience working with the Indonesian Government in this sector. Based on the Bank's
worldwide experience, GOI sought the Bank's advice on such issues as lower-cost alternatives
for junior secondary education, how to improve quality and how to tailor the proposed increased
access programs to local needs. The proposed project represents one in an overall program of
Bank support for expansion and quality improvements in basic education. Currently, the Bank is
actively preparing two other secondary education projects and the Government has requested that
the Bank provide both financial and technical assistance for future projects. The junior secondary
education development program is supported by other donors, including ADB and OECF. This
project complements the assistance to basic education provided by several ongoing projects:
(a)    Second Secondary Education and Management Project. This project (Loan
3158-IND), approved in 1990, consisted of: provision of quality inputs in
secondary schools (teacher training, equipment and books); a review and redesign
of the student assessment system; and a redesign of the information management
system. After initial problems, mainly due to the large national scope, the project
was restructured to operate in five pilot provinces, with satisfactory results. The
implementation experience from this project has provided the basis for the design
of the current project. In addition, the Bank is now very familiar with the issues
and the agencies involved in this subsector at the provincial level.
(b)   The Primary and Secondary School Teacher Development Projects. These
two projects (Loans 3496-IND and 3979-IND) address pre-service training of
primary and secondary school teachers respectively. They complement the
current project in the preparation and quality improvements of teachers and
substantial coordination between pre-service and in-service training is achieved
through the concurrent implementation of these projects. The Bank is also
heavily involved in ensuring that maximum coordination is achieved between
teacher development projects and the in-service training components of the
secondary education development projects.
(c)    The Primary Education Quality Improvement Project. Approved in 1992,
this project (Loan 3448-IND) provides quality inputs in primary schools in six
provinces. The issues related to primary schools that impact on transition,
enrollments and quality in junior secondary schools are being addressed in this
project on a limited scale. Project impact is now being reviewed, to determnine the
further strategies for implementation on a larger scale.
(d)   The Book and Reading Development Project. The project (Loan 3887-IND)
has recently started implementation (September 1995) to provide free textbooks
on a one-to-one basis to all primary and junior secondary education students in all



Staff Appraisal Report                            21
subjects. Books will be provided both to public and private schools. The project
provides one of the most essential quality inputs into basic education on a
systematic basis, and complements the secondary education development
projects, which will focus on supporting non-textbook educational materials and
teaching aids. Annex 9 provides details on Government and Bank strategies for
development of the sector.



22               Central Indonesia Junior Secondary Education Project
3.  THE PROJECT
A.    PROJECT OBJECTIVES
3.1    The project would assist the MOEC in addressing the quality and coverage of junior
secondary education in the provinces of Central Java, Yogyakarta, Central Kalimantan, and
West Kalimantan. The project's objectives are to:
(a)   expand access to junior secondary education in a cost-effective and equitable
way;
(b)   improve the quality of junior and senior secondary education; and
(c)   strengthen the management of the education system at all levels.
B.    PERFORMANCE INDICATORS AND CRITICAL SUCCESS FACTORS
3.2   Project impact will be assessed using the following indicators (See Annex 15):
(a)   achievement of project enrollment targets (satisfactory = 75 percent) in new
junior secondary schools and alternative programs by project completion date,
by province and district;
(b)    utilization of at least 75 percent of new capacity created by the project, by
province, district, subdistrict, and grade;
(c)   an increase in student examination scores, by province, district, subject, and
public and private school by an average of 0.2 points during the project; and
(d)   implementation of at least 80 percent of the work program as measured by the
physical targets and budget spent annually for project and routine activities in
each province.
C.    PROJECT DESCRIPTION
Project Components
3.3   To meet the objectives of increasing access to junior secondary education, improving
the quality of secondary education, and enhancing education management, activities related to
the following components and subcomponents would be implemented selectively and in
varying degrees in the public and private systems of the four project provinces. The total cost
of the project is $154.1 million, including contingencies.



StaffAppraisal Report                            23
3.4    Expand Access (estimated base costs of $60.3 million). To improve access to junior
secondary education, the project would (a) create a total of 45,000 school places by building
regular and small junior secondary schools in underserved rural areas; (b) gradually expand
alternative equivalency programs by creating 11,000 places with greater quality inputs for
those who cannot attend regular schools, and monitor the programs' effectiveness; (c) assist
in community construction of student and teacher accommodations for public and private
schools in isolated areas; (d) provide contract teachers for rural areas; (e) finance scholarships
for 34,000 poor students who perform well, with priority given to girls in public and private
schools; and (f) provide parents with information about how to take full advantage of the
benefits of junior secondary schooling in their local areas. See Annex 10 for more details on
options for increasing access. During negotiations the Government gave assurances that it
would (i) employ contract teachers, (ii) award scholarships, and (iii) select locations for the
construction of schools and dormitories, in accordance with criteria acceptable to the Bank.
3.5   Improve Quality (estimated base costs of $58.2 million). To improve the quality of
junior secondary education, the project would (a) strengthen school teachers' subject-matter
knowledge and pedagogical skills, including identification of learning difficulties, through
inservice and upgrading training programs, and support measures to bring teachers to rural
areas; (b) enhance the learning environment by providing schools with tools for teaching and
learning, including educational equipment and materials; science teaching aids; new teaching
rooms; and reference and library books; (c) support the development of school-level
initiatives to improve quality; and (d) further develop tests, examinations, and assessment
systems by training teachers in student evaluation, providing technical assistance, and
developing item databanks for school examinations. Project interventions would be aimed
particularly at public and private schools (including Madrasahs) in poor communities to
encourage continued private sector participation in the provision of education and to reduce
inequity in the project provinces. These access and quality interventions would help increase
access of girls in areas where gender disparities still exist.
3.6    Strengthen Education Management (estimated base costs of $5.9 million). To
strengthen the management of the education system, the project would (a) train school
principals and other administrators in managing schools and providing pedagogical support to
teachers; and (b) develop managerial and data analysis capacity to allow provincial and
district staff to effectively handle their increasing responsibilities for education planning and
management.
3.7    Support Project Management (estimated base costs of $7.0 million). The project
would support the strengthening of institutional capacities as well as the operational costs of
project management by the Kanwils, Kandeps/Kanin, and the MOEC directorate of secondary
education (Dikmenum).
Detailed Description of Interventions for each Province
3.8    Central Java (estimated base costs of $57.6 million). The project would complement
the efforts of the Government and donors in increasing enrollment in and improving the



24                Central Indonesia Junior Secondary Education Project
quality of junior secondary school, and particularly in meeting the needs of rural communities
where no school exists. Under the project, the province would create an additional 38,000
junior secondary school places in areas where enrollment rates are low.
3.9    Access. The project would build, furnish, and equip 77 new public SMPs of up to
nine classrooms to cater for an additional 28,000 students in large rural communities without
a public or private school. To target the poor, half of the new schools would be located in
villages classified as poor by the Government. In small rural communities, schools three- or
six-classroom schools may be more appropriate than nine-classroom schools. As part of total
project costs, the Government would acquire land required for the schools. The project would
provide contract teachers in rural schools as part of a pilot scheme to reduce teacher shortages
in the rural areas.
3.10   To reach potential students who cannot attend school because of direct and
opportunity costs, the project would fund an expansion of SMP Terbuka for an additional
6,000 students. The expansion would increase female access to schools. Emphasis would be
given to the provision of teaching and reference materials for students and teachers. Funding
for operational costs would allow teachers to attend class sessions and remove some of the
key obstacles to effective operations of this program. As in other project provinces,
intervention in the SMP Terbuka program would be modest and aimed at increasing its
quality while the program's effectiveness in teaching and retaining students is monitored and
evaluated.
3.11   To assist rural families who cannot afford to pay for education the project would
provide annual scholarships through GOI's scholarship program to approximately 24,600
low-income Central Java students who perform well in school. This program has a well
established selection procedure which involves various levels from the school to the Kanwil.
The disbursement of the funds will be directly to the recipient through the Provincial Pos Giro
Office. The project would also devote some funds for the development and production of
materials to sensitize parents to the benefits of junior secondary education and options for
schooling their children, particularly in areas where communities feel these are beyond their
reach.
3.12   Quality. To improve quality in public and private schools, Central Java would
concentrate on strengthening teacher skills and providing them with teaching resources. A
total of about 16,000 teachers would be given PKG inservice training. The aim would be to
develop a systematic link between the district and provincial trainers and school supervisors
to ensure that good teaching practices are routinized in the system. To reduce costs of
operation and logistical problems, training for most teachers would be moved from the
provincial headquarters to the district headquarters. For teachers in very remote areas, training
would be held in the provincial headquarters three times a year, followed by school visits by
teams who would cover multiple subjects. Funding would continue for the wide-reaching
