MGMP network of subject-matter teacher associations to provide teachers skills in use of
tests and identification of learning weaknesses. SMP Terbuka teachers and tutors would also
receive training.



Staff Appraisal Report                          25
3.13   The project would also provide a variety of teaching materials and equipment for
science and other subjects, about 3 million reference and library books for public and private
schools, and school office equipment. Science equipment would be provided in a variety of
packages to suit the diverse types and conditions of schools in the province. In addition,
libraries and administration/teacher rooms would be built at selected existing schools.
Support for expansion of quality public and private schools, especially religious schools in
rural communities would make it easier for girls to get access to junior secondary education
in a culturally acceptable way.
3.14   Education Management. Because of the sheer size of its education system in relation
to the other project provinces and its goal of rapidly expanding the system, Central Java
would need improved mechanisms and technology to manage its schools and analyze trends
for planning. To build local management capacity, the project would assist Kandep/Kanin and
Kanwil staff in developing information systems to analyze trends and identify problems. The
project would finance local and overseas technical assistance; incountry training; computers
and other equipment; the development, printing, and purchase of materials; office supplies;
and computer software. To support project management in Central Java, the project would
finance administrative operational costs and salaries, incountry training, and local and
overseas technical assistance.
3.15   Yogyakarta (estimated base costs of $25.2 million). As a result of its broad coverage
at the junior secondary level and its declining cohort population, Yogyakarta would focus on
improving quality rather than expanding access.  Innovative approaches in improving
schooling and teaching technologies would be implemented and evaluated, and the province
would participate in international comparisons of educational achievement.
3.16   Access.  No new schools or classrooms would be built under the project in
Yogyakarta, although specialized teaching rooms would be added to existing schools to
strengthen their teaching capacity (see Quality). The project would address remaining poverty
constraints to access by providing scholarships to 3,200 low-income students with high
academic achievement under the national scholarship program. The project would also
provide the public with information about the benefits of junior secondary education and
options for schooling their children, particularly in remaining pockets of lower enrollment.
3.17   Quality. Now that most young people are in school, Yogyakarta wants to improve
education quality to prepare them to respond to changes in the economy and society.
Yogyakarta's experience and lessons learned would serve to guide other parts of the country
in the future. To improve the quality of junior and senior secondary education, Yogyakarta
would implement several types of activities. First, the province would provide existing
schools with better teaching facilities, including specialized teaching rooms - including
computer rooms, language laboratories, science laboratories, libraries, and audiovisual aid
rooms - at selected schools. The second main area is staff development. About a third of the
province's public and private school teachers would receive either PKG training or continued
support through MGMP subject teacher associations. A majority of science and other subject
teachers and librarians would receive specialized training, including training in the use of
education technology. Third, the supply of instructional tools in public and private school



26               Central Indonesia Junior Secondary Education Project
classrooms -including reference and library books, science materials, audiovisual aids, and
computers -would be increased. In addition, new types of education equipment would be
gradually introduced and evaluated in selected schools through training and technical
assistance, and key staff would investigate emerging technologies used in other countries
through overseas training. Fourth, ongoing efforts in international as well as classroom
assessments of student performance, and academic projects of individual public and private
students and school teams, would be supported.
3.18   Education Management. Because of the new directions Yogyakarta is taking to
improve quality, the provincial education staff's ability to evaluate the effectiveness of
introduced measures is especially important. The project would also assist Yogyakarta in its
new management and planning functions. To improve management of schools, school
administrators would receive management training. The project would finance local and
overseas technical assistance; incountry training; computers and other equipment; the
development, printing, and purchase of materials; office supplies; and computer software. To
support project management, the project would finance administration operational costs and
salaries, incountry training, and local and foreign technical assistance.
3.19   Central Kalimantan (estimated base costs of $20.3 million). The scale of the Central
Kalimanitan's expansion of school places would be small, but would be aimed at difficult
areas with poor populations where adequate private schooling is not viable. The project
would also aim to improve the quality of rural schools and provide open schooling for a
limited number of students who cannot attend regular schools.
3.20   Access. Under the project, Central Kalimantan would create about 8,400 school
places. To increase access in rural areas with small, scattered populations, the project would
build, furnish, and equip 53 SMP Kecils ("small SMPs") with three classrooms, accounting
for about three-quarters of new places provided under the project. The Government would
acquire land for these schools as part of total project costs. Districts with low enrollment rates
and villages classified as poor would receive priority. The main advantage of the SMP Kecil
is that it would allow the services of traditional schools to be provided to underserved areas of
poverty that have relatively small numbers of potential students, particularly girls, in the
catchment area. To help attract and retain good teachers and principals in such areas, grants
for community construction of a total of 160 residences also would be supported under the
project for public and private schools. For some remote areas that have high drop-out rates
and irregular attendance because of difficulties in commuting to school, the project would
provide grants for community construction of 40 dormitories. This effort would be provided
selectively to communities where there is good justification and where contribution from the
community is evident. For schools that have insufficient teachers and have trouble attracting
staff, contract teachers would be hired. The project would also modestly expand the SMP
Terbuka program through the provision of learning and reference books to about 2,000
students and teachers, and by financing operating costs. Scholarships would be provided to
2,000 low-income students per year. The project would also provide for the development of
public information about the benefits of junior secondary education and local options for
schooling.



Staff Appraisal Report                           27
3.21   Quality. To improve teacher skills and knowledge, the project would provide
inservice PKG training to about 1,225 teachers, as well as MGMP and other training to 1,450
of the province's 4,300 teachers, including training in content of core subjects, identification
of learning weaknesses, and classroom assessment methods. In addition, teacher instructors
would receive training in subject areas to strengthen the PKG program in the province. For
remote areas, the PKG program would be modified to local circumstances - for example, by
financing three two-week courses annually and teams to spend two or more days helping not
just teachers in PKG training but all teachers at a remote school. The program would also
integrate principals into the training program so they can provide systematic onservice
support to teachers in their schools. To improve the quality of science instruction, the project
would provide science equipment to both public and private schools in rural areas. All
schools in these areas would be classified to determine their potential for using different types
of equipment, and a variety of packages would be developed to suit their needs. The majority
of schools in the project, which are generally rural and poor, would receive fairly simple
equipment. These schools would also receive other teaching aids and equipment. The project
would provide about 600,000 reference and library books and would build libraries at 18
existing schools without them. Examinations item banks, storing of examinations, and
techniques for analyzing learning weaknesses would be developed through local technical
assistance to improve student achievement and to feed the results of student assessment back
to the classroom level. Annex I I provides details of these programs.
3.22   In this province, the addition of student and teacher accommodation would help
promote access of girls in areas where this is still a problem. It would be easier to attract and
retain female teachers and students in these areas especially when combined with
scholarships, contract teacher positions and quality improvement of private/community
schools.
3.23   Education Management. To strengthen the province's ability to take on additional
planning and management responsibility, the project would assist Kanwil and Kandep/Kanin
staff in developing and making better use of its information systems. The project would also
strengthen the flow of usable information among schools and education authorities in schools,
districts, provinces, and Jakarta, in part by expanding the use of technologies that reduce the
distance barrier in isolated areas. School principals would be provided with school
management training. To improve educational management, the project would finance local
and overseas technical assistance; incountry training; computers and other equipment; the
development, printing, and purchase of materials; office supplies; and computer software. To
support project management in Central Kalimantan, the project would finance administrative
operational costs and salaries, incountry training and local and overseas technical assistance.
3.24   West Kalimantan (estimated base costs of $23.5 million). Like Central Kalimantan,
West Kalimantan would have to implement a wide variety of strategies to improve access and
quality in areas of low population density. The project would expand enrollment in remote
and low income areas by 14,000 places through the construction of smaller schools and the
modest expansion of nonconventional education.



28               Central Indonesia Junior Secondary Education Project
3.25  Access. West Kalimantan faces many of the same problems of access as Central
Kalimantan, though the population density is higher and the private school system is more
extensive. The province can begin to take advantage of improving transportation networks.
For remote areas of poverty that the private sector does not and is unlikely to cover, the
project would fund the construction and furnishing of 50 three-classroom SMP Kecils, which
would serve 6,000 male and female students. For more populated rural areas and sites that
are better served by road or water, the province would build, furnish, and equip 20 six-
classroom SMPs, which would serve 4,800 students. The cost to Government of acquiring
land for schools would be included in total project costs. The project would conduct
feasibility studies for SMP Terbuka and create about 3,000 Terbuka male and female student
places where distance or cost is a constraint, though the provision of educational materials
and operations funds. For students who are willing to live away from home to attend the
nearest school, grants for community construction of 20 student dormitories would be
supported where there is a demonstrated interest. For schools that do not have enough
qualified teachers, the project would finance the hiring of contract teachers for limited periods
and support grants for community construction of 34 teacher accommodations to attract
permanent teachers. Scholarships under the national program would be extended annually to
4,200 low-income students with high achievement. The project would also provide for the
development of information to inform the public about the benefits of junior secondary
education and local schooling opportunities.
3.26   Quality. To improve teacher quality, West Kalimantan is giving particular attention
to its rural public and private school teachers, many of whom are undertrained and need
ongoing support. About 360 teachers would receive PKG training and another 1,800 would
receive local MGMP training in subject areas. Because many of its rural teachers start from a
weak base, West Kalimantan would upgrade teachers without the DIII qualification (those
with DI and DII) through Open University courses. The project would support efforts to
retain teachers in rural areas not only by providing more training and better housing (see
above), but also by financing and studying the benefits of an existing teacher rotation scheme
designed to bring about more balanced and equitable distribution of teachers. To improve the
quality and effectiveness of the expanding SMP Terbuka program, its tutors and
administrators would be upgraded. As in Central Kalimantan, the project would also provide
reference and library books, simple laboratory equipment, and teaching materials to improve
quality at both public and private schools. New libraries would be built at 15 selected existing
schools.  Local technical assistance would support the development of examinations
databases, educational studies, and evaluations, and to encourage greater use of student
assessment at the school level. As in Central Kalimantan, the access and quality interventions
would help reduce gender disparities in participation in junior secondary education where this
still exists.
3.27   Education Management. Like Central Kalimantan, West Kalimantan faces challenges
in monitoring and maintaining two-way communications with schools in remote areas. The
project would assist Kandeps/Kanin, and Kanwils, and the central MOEC in developing
better information systems for monitoring and planning. School administrators would also
receive inservice training. To support these interventions, the project would finance local and
overseas technical assistance; incountry training; computers and other equipment; the



Staff Appraisal Report                           29
development, printing, and purchase of materials; office supplies; and computer software to
support. To support project management in West Kalimantan, the project would finance
administrative operational costs and salaries, incountry training, and local and overseas
technical assistance.
3.28   Central Management Support (estimated base costs of $4.8 million). The project
would support this coordinating and advisory role of the central MOEC directorate for
primary and secondary education in Jakarta. The project would fund technical assistance,
local staff training, and operational costs for project management, public information,
monitoring, supervision, and evaluation for these offices. This coordinating unit, funded
initially under this project with supplementary funding as necessary under future projects,
would also assist with the implementation of other proposed Bank-funded projects under the
junior secondary education program, particularly the East Java and East Nusa Tenggara
Junior Secondary Education Project. Computers and the development and maintenance of
Dikmenum's management information systems would also be provided, particularly to
enhance its communications and joint planning efforts with each of the Kanwils.
Project Supportfor Private Education
3.29   The project would devote a substantial amount of resources to improve the quality
and management of private education. This would help reduce the disparity between public
and private education and encourage parents to view private schooling as a worthwhile
option. Specifically, private schools would benefit directly from: (a) funding of contract
teachers for rural schools; (b) teacher in-service training; (c) provision of teaching equipment
and materials; (d) provision of reference and library books; (e) provision of building materials
for community construction of student dormitories and teacher residences at isolated schools
in Kalimantan; (f) management training for school principals; and (g) support for innovative
quality initiatives to be piloted in Yogyakarta province. In addition, private schools would
benefit indirectly from such project activities as improved Kanwil management, monitoring,
and evaluation of schools and students; and the construction of public schools, whose
facilities are often used after-hours for private schooling through teacher associations. The
effectiveness of public assistance would be increased by reducing abuse of public support
through better monitoring of schools. Self-supporting "equivalent" private schools and rent-
seeking private schools would not receive project funding, while viable schools with the
potential to increase enrollment and quality would receive support at about twice the level of
previous Government efforts.
3.30 These efforts are in line with Government policy (explicitly stated in Repelita VI) that
private schools should maintain and even increase their share of total junior secondary
enrollments. The public sector already devotes significant amounts of resources to support
and improve the quality of private schools.  Government support currently includes
secondment of public sector teachers to private schools, distribution of approved textbooks
free of charge, and operational cost subsidies to private schools at the lower end of financial
viability. Funding is usually lower for quality inputs such as teacher training and distribution
of equipment, so project financing in such areas would expand the regular program (See
Annex 12).



30                Central Indonesia Junior Secondary Education Project
D.  PROJECT ECONOMIC ANALYSIS
3.31   The economic analysis conducted for the project consisted of: (a) analysis of
educational demand and the rationale for public investment in junior secondary education;
(b) fiscal analysis; (c) review of alternatives; and (d) cost-benefit analysis. Details are
provided in Annex 7.
3.32   There are both efficiency and equity grounds for additional public intervention in
junior secondary education in Indonesia today. It is primarily the poor and near-poor (and
mostly those in rural areas) who are not enrolling in school and whose participation declined
in the late 1980s to early 1990s. One of the primary factors explaining the low demand during
that period is the high direct costs of education. A second factor is indirect (opportunity) costs
which exacerbated the effect of high direct costs. Coupled with stagnant real wages in the
1980s and the effects of cuts in public expenditure on basic education in the mid-1980s
(school construction came to a virtual halt), these factors contributed to faltering demand. The
past two years have seen the reversal of these trends as enrollment rates have risen
significantly in all provinces.
3.33   Since investment in junior secondary education is desirable on both efficiency and
equity grounds, the key questions for the Government are the budgetary implications of the
expansion and how to expand the system. The budgetary implications of the expansion are
sizable ($7.6 billion; see Annex 7 and para. 1.9), but estimation shows that the policy is
attainable if the economy continues to grow at the rate attained in recent decades, additional
funds are made available to junior secondary including external borrowing (para. 1.9), and the
private sector retains its current significant share.
3.34   Analysis of alternatives considered the role of the private sector in the expansion
program and the fiscal implications of mainly public expansion versus one which made room
for private expansion at the current rate of 40 percent of enrollments in private schools. The
budgetary implications double in the first scenario ($14.5 billion compared to $7.6 billion).
There are additional merits to preserving the private sector. First, given the current and
projected surplus of teachers, a robust private sector is an effective means for helping to clear
the market for teachers. Second, Madrasah schools (religious schools which currently enroll
about 20 percent of junior secondary students) offer access to junior secondary in small, poor
communities and at a cost that is significantly lower than even MOEC-supervised private
schools. These schools also enroll a higher proportion of girls than boys, reflecting the
preference pattern of some families for non-secular education. Third, maintaining the share of
private schools at the junior secondary level establishes the basis for sustaining the even
larger share of private schools at the senior secondary level (54 percent of enrollments in
1993/94).



StaffAppraisal Report                              31
3.35   Beyond the public/private alternatives, the Government has experimented with
alternative modes of delivering junior secondary education (in respect of the regular SMP
model). These include SMP Kecil and SMP Terbuka. While not entirely substitutes for each
other (or to the regular SMPs), and experimentation to improve their quality is still ongoing,
preliminary unit cost comparisons indicate that gains can be achieved by utilizing these less
costly alternative models of junior secondary education in rural and remote areas.
3.36   The economic rate of return for the project investment is estimated by province and
component (increasing access and enhancing quality, but excluding the management
component). The estimates are obtained by solving for the internal rate of return that equates
a stream of benefits and costs resulting from the investment over a thirty year horizon. The
benefits of the projects are derived from the increased numbers of students enrolled in junior
secondary in any given year, but also include the additional stream of students expected to
proceed to senior secondary and tertiary education. A monetary value of the benefits of each
stream of beneficiaries is estimated using the difference in wages between one level of
education and the next. Benefits will also accrue from investments in enhancing the quality of
junior secondary. A benchmark assumption of a 15 percent additional wage premium earned
by a higher quality graduate is made. High returns were found for both quality and access
components, with returns on quality enhancements generally higher than for expansion of
access, particularly in Central Kalimantan and West Kalimantan, as noted in Table 3.1 below:
Table 3.1
Rates of Return to Project Investments by Component and Province
C.omponent     Central Java    Yogyakarta    Central Kalimantan  West Kalimantan
Access              18.0           14.3*            12.1               13.8
Quality             17.7           16.5             20.3               22.9
Total               18.0           15.8             14.8               17.2
+ Provision of scholarships to poor and capable students. No additional building of school places will be carried out by project.
3.37   Simulations were run varying some of the underlying assumptions. In all, the main
variables to which the system appears to be most sensitive to are those affecting quality. This
is not surprising given the large numbers of beneficiaries which are impacted by this
investment. Sensitivity analysis reveals, for example, that (a) the rate of return for quality
inputs declines from 23 to 18 percent in West Kalimantan if only a 10 percent increase in
additional wage premium for higher quality graduates is assumed; (b) the rate of return
declines from 23 to 20 percent if incremental recurrent costs for quality improvements in
West Kalimantan increases from $8 per student to $10; and (c) the rate of return for access
inputs in Central Java increases from 18 to 19 percent if the junior secondary-primary wage
differential increases by 20 percent.



32               Central Indonesia Junior Secondary Education Project
Impact on Poverty
3.38   Those not enrolled in junior secondary today are predominantly from the lowest four
income deciles. The project will reach these poor and near-poor through its poverty-targeted
sub-components. Scholarships will be awarded annually to approximately 34,000 poor (and
capable) students. Smaller schools in Kalimantan and regular schools in Central Java, a total
of 45,000 school places, will be built in rural areas, with priority given to the poorest villages.
Another 11,000 places in alternative programs, which are free to parents, will benefit areas
where direct and opportunity costs are highest. In all, the project will contribute to offsetting
the geographical and financial obstacles barring poorer children from junior secondary
schools, thus reducing social inequality in education access.
Impact on Women
3.39   Enrollment figures by province reveal no great disparities between male and female
participation in junior secondary education in the four project provinces. In 1994/95, females
accounted for 48-50 percent of total enrollment in the provinces and about 49 percent of the
cohort populations. Kabupaten-level data suggest that there may be somewhat more
significant disparities in certain pockets of the project provinces, particularly in rural areas.
In some kabupaten of Central Java and Central Kalimantan, for example, females account for
42 to 45 percent of total enrollment. Distance from home to school is one of the main reasons
for local disparities - parents are often reluctant to allow their daughters to travel far to
school. The following components can be expected to improve female access to junior
secondary schools: (a) expansion of school places in rural areas, where female
participation is lowest; (b) significant expansion of the SMP Kecil in rural communities
in the Kalimantan provinces; (c) expansion of SMP Terbuka programs in Kalimantan and
Central Java; (d) scholarships to low-income students, with priority given to girls; and (e)
quality improvements at private schools, including Madrasahs which usually has a higher
female enrollment.
E.  PROJECT MANAGEMENT AND IMPLEMENTATION
Institutional Background
3.40   Project design and the proposed management arrangements for this project are firmly
rooted in lessons of experience from earlier Bank-financed education projects, especially the
Second Secondary Education and Management Project (Loan 3158-[ND), which included a
pilot operation on which the design of the proposed project is based. During preparation the
emphasis has been on institutional changes: (a) strengthening participatory features;
(b) strengthening province-level implementation with respect to sector analysis, project
design and implementation; and (c) strengthening the national-level implementation capacity.
Because of the importance of these elements for project design, they are summarized below
and discussed in detail in Annex 13.



StaffAppraisal Report                            33
Participatory Features
3.41   Beneficiary participation has been a major element of project design. A milestone
was reached in this respect in December 1994 when the Secondary Education Directorate
(Dikmenum) with Bank assistance conducted a two-day seminar with a large group of
stakeholders. This seminar helped: (a) define the objectives of the earlier pilot project and its
successor (the proposed project); (b) review options for project management structures and
responsibilities; (c) review options for implementation procedures; and (d) especially, seek a
consensus among seminar participants on implementation of the above.
3.42   Subsequent sectoral analysis and project development under the proposed project has
built on experiences from this workshop. Several workshops on sectoral issues, project
design and institutional framework have subsequently been conducted at different times and
locations. The result is a series of provincially organized project proposals that have been
developed with major involvement from the stakeholders: province- and district-level
education officers (Kanwils and Kandeps/Kanin), parent organizations (BP3), schools and
educational societies (yayasans).
3.43   Beneficiary participation will continue throughout project implementation. During
implementation, communities will participate in project development through regular
meetings at the sub-district level attended by members of parents' associations (BP3), school
principals, village chiefs, religious leaders, and community social-service workers. These
meetings will propose and evaluate possible project activities appropriate to local conditions
and consistent with local needs. Sub-district proposals would be submitted through the
Kandep/Kanin to the Provincial Project Implementation Unit (PPIU) for its review against
agreed criteria and possible inclusion in the project.
3.44   A key concept of the proposed project is that detailed annual work programs would
be prepared each year. The costs and details of the outer implementation years would not be
considered firm until an annual budgeting and evaluation process has taken place through a
bottom-up planning process each implementation year. This process is consistent with
existing Government procedures, but under the proposed project the involvement of project
beneficiaries in this process would be explicitly strengthened.
Province-Level Implementation
3.45   Provincial Structure. Provincial involvement in sectoral analysis and project design
has shown that there is a rapidly growing capacity to implement the proposed project. During
preparation, Kanwil staff conducted sectoral analyses, commissioned preparation reports from
lower administrative tiers, and appraised and revised these reports prior to discussions with
Bank missions. Because of these rapidly developing human capabilities, the project would be
implemented through existing line authorities at the Kanwil level. There would be no
expansion of staff positions, and the administrative structures would remain unchanged.
Project-specific task forces, commensurate with the focus and complexity of each of the four
provincial sub-projects, would be established and disbanded as and when necessary during
project implementation. This concept is not only viable: it embodies the ongoing concept of



34                Central Indonesia Junior Secondary Education Project
empowering and engaging provincial staff in sectoral analysis and project preparation. (See
Annex 13 for Terms of Reference and the organizational structure for project management at
the provincial level.)
3.46   To lend a focal point to the operation, the Kakanwil, would be the Project Director. A
senior staff (such as Kabid-Dikmenum or someone under his/her supervision) would be the
Project Manager and would be responsible directly for implementation and management of
the project. A high-level Provincial Project Advisory Committee, headed by the head of the
Kanwil would give guidance to project implementors.  The committee would include
members from the senior-most tiers of the public and private provincial education system and
other Government agencies (see Annex 13 for membership). A technical resource group in
each province would assist in technical matters. It would have access to consultants and other
expertise as required.
3.47   Under the PPIU there would typically be the following five divisions to carry out the
following functions: (a) Procurement and Civil Works; (b) Quality Improvement;
(c) Accounting; (d) Progress Monitoring; and (e) Supervision and Evaluation. During
negotiations, the Government gave assurances that it would establish by November 1, 1996
(i) a Provincial Project Advisory Committee and (ii) a Provincial Project Implementation
Unit in each of the project provinces.
3.48   Review and Criteria for Annual Project Activities. The  PPIU would review
submitted proposals against explicit and well-advertised criteria. On completion of its review
in October each year, the PPIU would: (a) discuss the technical merits and scope of the
proposals with a Bank mission; and (b) submit the results and the proposed next-year budget
to the Provincial Project Advisory Committee for its approval.  Before submission to
Bappenas, the Provincial Project Advisory Committee would submit the proposal for
endorsement by the national education authorities in Jakarta to ensure consistency with
agreed Government and Bank policies, criteria, procedures and project magnitudes.
3.49   Selection  criteria would  include: (i) completeness; (ii) relevance; (iii) cost-
effectiveness; (iv) sustainability; and (v) implementability. Proposals would be reviewed for
completeness to test whether they are sufficiently developed to constitute sub-projects with
clearly defined objectives, inputs, costs, timetables and action plans. The relevance of the
submitted proposals would be tested against any of the stated objectives of the project,
especially poverty reduction, and taking into account known implementation constraints. The
cost-effectiveness merits of submitted proposals would be tested against alternatives for
delivering comparable educational service. The long-term sustainability of the proposals
would be tested against their implications for recurrent costs in the Government's budget, and
their likely implications for long-term effects on households in the affected communities.
The implementability of the submitted proposal would be reviewed against institutional,
managerial and other non-financial constraints.



Staff Appraisal Report                        35
National-level Implementation
3.50   Guided by a Central Program Advisory Committee, Dikmenum would be the
principal national level project implementation agency (see Annex 13 for proposed
organizational structure and Terms of Reference). Its role would be that of project oversight
and facilitation -- not implementation.  Dikmenum  would: (a) ensure that provincial
implementation focuses on achieving agreed project objectives; (b) assist the provinces in
establishing appropriate annual work plans; (c) help ensure timely preparation of regional
project budgets (DIP); (d) assist in establishing appropriate management information systems
at Kanwil and national levels; (e) disseminate pertinent information to the provinces; and (f)
identify or provide technical assistance to the provinces to ensure compliance with agreed
project-specific rules and regulations.
3.51   Dikmenum would help design and implement national and provincial capacity-
building plans as the project unfolds. This would include establishing appropriate project
information systems and procedures on the principle that relevant information from one
system would feed into the next system, and that there would be meaningful back and forth
flow of information across administrative levels. These would include: (a) budgeting
systems; (b) accounting; (c) procurement; (d) process indicators; and (e) project outcome
indicators. During negotiations the Government gave assurances that it would establish by
November 1, 1996 (i) a Central Program Advisory Committee and (ii) a Central Program
Coordination Unit.
3.52   Budgeting Systems. All project budgets (the DIP -- Government and Bank) would
be channeled annually to the provinces in accordance with normal Government investment
budgeting procedures.  The provinces would arrange payments to contractors through
standard Government procedures. Payments would be authorized by provincial Treasury
offices of the Ministry of Finance in accordance with existing well-established procedures.
During negotiations the Government provided assurances that it would, not later than
October I each year (beginning in 1997), furnish to the Bank for comments a report on the
implementation of the budget for the past and current fiscal years, and the proposed budget
for the following fiscal year.
3.53   Project accounts. Accounts would be established in such a way that meaningful
information about unit costs of project activities could be obtained as the project progresses.
To assist in this, an Indonesian accounting firm would be hired for a period of about three
months.
3.54   Procurement Documents and Procedures. The Central Program Coordination Unit
(CPCU) in Dikmenum would establish appropriate procurement documents and procedures
under the proposed project and provide appropriate technical assistance in this respect to the
provinces. This process has started with respect to the procurement documents for equipment
which were developed under the pilot part of the Second Secondary Education and
Management Project (Loan 3158-IND).



36               Central Indonesia Junior Secondary Education Project
3.55  Project Implementation Handbook.  For the day-by-day practice of project
implementation, the CPCU, PPIU and Kandep/Kanin staff will rely upon a Project
Implementation Handbook. Written in Bahasa Indonesia, the Handbook will describe in
details and in concrete terms the methodology, procedures and multiple steps of project
implementation and project monitoring. Its content will draw mostly from the Staff Appraisal
Report and Legal Documents, GOI and Bank procurement and disbursement guidelines, and
will seek to emulate the best practices in the country. The Handbook is expected to: (a)
become the key and uniform point of reference for all levels of project management; (b)
provide users with ready answer for problem solving; and (c) offset the relative lack of
experience with project management at the provincial level. Dikmenum is coordinating the
preparation of the handbook.
F.    MONITORING AND EVALUATION
Performance Indicators
3.56   Process Indicators. An Implementation Monitoring System would in part be fed by
information from the accounting system. It would also include information about non-
financial progress in the project, such as deliveries of educational materials and numbers of
teachers trained.
3.57   Project Outcome Indicators.  The outcomes of the proposed project would be
monitored throughout its implementation through four key indicators. In addition to the
above education indicators, the efficiency of various parts of the system would be measured
through ongoing econometric studies.
3.58   The project's performance indicators (Annex 15) were discussed and agreed with the
Government during negotiations. Progress in implementing the project is to be assessed
through monitoring indicators and formative evaluation using the detailed performance
indicators. Additionally outcome indicators have been identified to guide development
impact evaluation. Dikmenum would undertake the monitoring and formative evaluation as
well as required technical audits. The above indicators would also form the basis of
supervision by the Bank (the supervision plan is summarized in Annex 16).
Reports
3.59   Progress Reports. The PPIU in each of the project provinces would prepare half
yearly reports on project implementation which would include a description of
implementation of each component including discussion of any issues, delays or changes; an
updated list of performance indicators; actions on recommendations of the previous Bank
Supervision Missions; a list of all contract activities, and a set of tables covering:
expenditures, training and technical assistance activities, civil works activities, and an update
of project the implementation schedule. Annex 14 provides guidelines for preparation of the
progress report.



Staff Appraisal Report                          37
3.60   Annual and Midterm Reviews. The timing of the annual review will be coordinated
with the annual budget preparation cycle. The first review will cover the period up to March
31, 1998. These reviews will summarize the status of project interventions, project activities
on the basis of project indicators, major implementation problems, and the revised
implementation plan for the following year. The midterm review and assessment will be in
October 1999. During negotiations, the Government provided assurances that it would
implement policies and procedures to enable adequate project monitoring and evaluation in
accordance with agreed indicators and (i) prepare a report by July 31 each year (beginning
in 1997) on monitoring and evaluation activities during the previous year and
recommendations for the following year; (ii) prepare a midterm report on or about July 31,
1999; and (iii) discuss these reports with the Bank by October 31 of the same fiscal year.
3.61   Implementation Completion Report. At the completion of the project, MOEC
would compile an Implementation Completion Report within six months of the closing date
of the loan. This report will summarize, among other things, (a) program activities in terms
of input, process, and output indicators; (b) an assessment of project activities; (c) the
project's financial status; and (d) implementation issues and recommendations.  During
Negotiations, the Government agreed to furnish to the Bank within six months after the
project closing date a plan designed to ensure the continued effectiveness of the project
objectives.
3.62   Technical Audits. To ensure accountability to the Government, the World Bank,
schools, and the general public, a technical audit of the various project components would be
performed annually. The technical audit would be carried out by an independent consultant
with no association with the project and employing specialists with expertise in (a) secondary
education administration and finance in Indonesia; (b) the management and distribution of
project inputs; (c) school construction; and (d) educational equipment and materials. The
goal of the technical audits would be to provide an independent and impartial assessment of
the quality and adequacy of procedures and processes employed by MOEC in Jakarta and the
project provinces.  The audit team would examine the efficiency and effectiveness of
implementation by the provinces and the support provided by the center. The team would
also assess to what extent project inputs are being distributed to target areas and groups
according to agreed criteria. The team would examine whether Kanwil and Kandep/Kanin
staff are performing as reported, whether they have the skills required, and whether
equipment and technical assistance provided are adequate and being used as reported. The
audits would include a sample check of documentation and selected site visits (about two per
year) to compare reality in the field against reports submitted and to ensure compliance with
accounting, procurement, and reporting guidelines. At negotiations, the Government provided
assurances that it would carry out annual technical audits of project implementation and
provide them to the Bank no later than six months after the end of the fiscal year.
3.63   Studies. Evaluation studies of the project processes and outcomes would be included
in the project to enable GOI and the Bank to assess the impact and cost effectiveness of this
project and related projects. A monitoring and evaluation expert will help GOI design the
studies to help measure the effect of the project's quality and access components on target



38               Central Indonesia Junior Secondary Education Project
populations. Other measures that would be employed include user surveys and studies by
anthropologists on the effect and appropriateness of project interventions in local settings.



Staff Appraisal Report                            39
4. PROJECT COSTS AND FINANCING
A. PROJECT COSTS
4.1    The total cost of the project is estimated at US$154.1 million equivalent (Rp 359.3 billion),
including price contingencies and identifiable taxes and duties. Tables 4.1 and 4.2 summarize the
estimated costs by project component and by category of expenditure. Annex 18 shows further
details on project costs.
4.2    Bases of Cost Estimates. Base costs are estimated using April 1996 prices. Construction
costs are based on the most recent cost estimates per square meter of simple construction that the
Government planning ministry (Bappenas) publishes each year for each district (kabupaten). An
allowance of 8 percent over the latest (1994) figures has been included to update the estimates.
Science and office equipment costs are based on recent costs available from the Second Secondary
Education and Management Project. Learning and teaching materials costs are based on similar items
recently procured in other education projects in Indonesia. Costs for domestic consultants are in line
with recent education projects for appropriately qualified experts. Costs of travel, per diem and
honoraria are consistent with current Government figures and recent education projects and have been
agreed between the Bank and the Government. Costs for short-term in-country training are derived
from experiences in the Second Secondary Education and Management Project. These costs vary
widely by the type of training, travel distance and training, ranging from $35 to $200, averaging
about US$50. Scholarship grants are about $130 per person-year.
4.3    Contingency Allowances. The project consists of a large number of items of small
monetary value. Because the exact number of these items is not vital to the success of the project, no
physical contingencies have been included. Price contingencies (US$22.8 million equivalent, which
represents 17% of total baseline costs) have been calculated at 8.7 percent per year for local costs
and 2.4 percent per year for international costs.
4.4    Foreign Exchange Costs. Foreign exchange costs have been estimated at US$35.9 million
(representing about 23 percent of total costs). These costs are based on the following percentages:
(a) construction and fumiture, 30 percent; (b) equipment, 70 percent; (c) foreign consultants, 80
percent, (d) in-country training, learning and teaching materials and travel costs, 15 percent; and
(e) local consultancies, 10 percent.
4.5    Taxes and Duties. Identifiable taxes and duties are estimated at US$6.5 million equivalent
based on the current 10 percent value added tax on all goods and materials. It would be financed
entirely by the Government of Indonesia.

B. FINANCING
4.6    Financing Plan. The project would be financed by a proposed loan, covering 70 percent of
total project costs excluding taxes (100 percent foreign and 58 percent of local expenditures). The
Government would finance the balance of project costs including taxes from its annual budgets. The
incremental recurrent costs of the project would represent a negligible share of the education budget
of the project provinces; representing 1 percent for Central Java and less than 2 percent for both West
and Central Kalimantan (Annex 7). As there will be no expansion in Yogyakarta, the incremental
cost would be insignificant, related only to the quality of education.

C. PROCUREMENT
4.7    Organization and Management of Procurement. The procurement section of the Kanwil
Office in each province would be in charge of procurement under the general guidance of the
designated project manager (Pimpro) in each province. Whenever necessary (such as in the case of
conducting ICB), the procurement section at the provincial (Kanwil) level could request the
assistance of specialized expertise from the MOEC Secondary Education Directorate (Dikmenum) in
Jakarta.
4.8    With the exception of community-based construction (which would be organized by the
communities themselves), all construction activities would be organized through the Public Works
offices mostly in rural areas. These would be for small, scattered construction contracts totaling
/a Includes expenditures for travel, per diem, allowances and consumable materials, but excludes GOI staff
salaries for project implementation



Staff Appraisal Report                              45
about 800 contracts. About 200 of these contracts would be for 3-9 room schools, the remaining 600
contracts would be smaller still. About 75 percent of these contracts would be less than US$50,000
equivalent. All contracts would be carried out through the use of procurement procedures and
documentation acceptable to the Bank at the provincial level. For contracts between US$50,000 and
$200,000 these rules would be National Competitive Bidding (NCB) procedures based on the Bank's
Standard Bidding Documents for Small Works, translated into Bahasa Indonesia. Bidding against a
known reference price would be permitted and foreign bidders would be allowed to participate.
Procurement of small works estimated to cost less than US$50,000, would be carried under local
procurement rules with which all participants in Indonesia are familiar. Despite the fact that these
rules deviate in some respects from the Bank's preferred rules, they are acceptable to the Bank in this
case, given: (a) the tiny size of these contracts; (b) their scattered nature on hundreds of project sites;
and (c) the fact that these works would be carried out two administrative levels down from the
Central Government in Jakarta.
4.9    A built-in procurement supervisory function (para 4.11) would help ensure that the bid
openings would be public and that all the agreed procurement rules would be carried out. With
respect to bid methodology, the planning directorate in Jakarta (Bappenas) keeps and publicizes an
annually updated list of reference prices for the m2 construction of different types of construction in
each district (kabupaten). These unit prices include site works and fees related to the management,
design and supervision of construction. Because the bidding documents spell out the number of
square meters to be constructed and the unit costs (per m2) are known, the bidding is done against a
known reference price. With respect to bid evaluation, only those bids that are in the "brackets"
between the reference price plus 10 percent (top), and 90 percent of the reference price (bottom) are
considered to be responsive bids. Other bids are rejected as non-responsive. This practice would be
accepted for all contracts to be awarded under the project (all of which are expected to be less than
$200,000).
4.10   Construction Procedures and Criteria. Rules for the size and nature of construction items
to be included in the project have been tentatively agreed with the Borrower. These include, that
when building new schools or adding rooms to existing ones, the ratio of auxiliary rooms to the total
number of classrooms at each school would be as follows: Three room schools would be entitled to
receive a small administrative unit. In such schools the teachers room would double as a library. Six
room schools would be entitled to a standard-size administration block and a library. "Full-sized"
(nine-classroom) schools would follow standard Government designs. The design of other rooms
would also be in accordance with the existing, publicized standard designs for such rooms.
4.11   Construction Procurement Oversight. The construction activities for the Project would be
supervised from Dikmenum in Jakarta by a local consulting architect/engineer who would be hired by
this project for the duration of all the project. This project-hired supervisor would be central to the
success of the construction components. The supervisor would be a technical link between local
monitors in the procurement section in each Kanwil, the PCU in Jakarta and the Bank, including in
particular its procurement staff in the Resident Mission in Indonesia (RSI). The TORs of this person
would be to: (a) ensure that the agreed procurement rules are understood and adhered to by all
provincial parties; (b) train Kanwil-level education staff in monitoring and reporting on construction-
related activities; (c) keep PWD technical staff in each kabupaten informed about the Bank's
requirements with respect to procurement of construction; and (d) review the bidding procedures and



46                 Central Indonesia Junior Secondary Education Project
contract awards of about 110 contracts over the life of the project, and determine that it was
implemented in accordance with agreed the agreed rules and proceduresa/.
4.12   Procurement Methods. All procurement of goods and works would be done in accordance
with the Bank's Guidelines for procurement under IBRD Loans and IDA Credits, dated January 1995
and revised in January 1996. The loan proceeds would be used to finance the procurement of goods,
works and services as follows:
4.13   School construction activities, totaling about US$25.0 million for contracts equal to or
exceeding US$50,000 each would be procured under NCB procedures acceptable to the Bank.
Contracts estimated to cost less than US$50,000 each and totaling US$27.0 million would be
procured from domestic contractors under lump-sum, fixed-price contracts awarded on the basis of
written invitations to at least three qualified contractors, or through a publicly extended invitation to
qualified domestic contractors. The head of the procurement section in the Kandep office of the
MOEC in each province would negotiate annually with the public works department (at the DTNAS-
level) to have the annually agreed works carried out within each year's budget period.
4.14   In many parts of Kalimantan, the least cost alternative to retain teachers and students is
through dormitory construction. In the interest of the sustainability of the project, accommodation
construction activities (totaling about US$5.9 million) would be carried out under the auspices of
community organizations (such as BP3). This refers specifically to the construction of project-
financed teacher and student residences in parts of Kalimantan. Communities that meet agreed
criteria for such construction (see Attachment to Annex 10) would be re-imbursed for the cost of
materials. All labor would be provided by the community.
4.15   Procurement of equipment, furniture and consumable materials for contracts equal to or
exceeding US$200,000 each, and totaling about US$10.0 million would be carried out through ICB,
using the Bank's Standard Bidding Documents for the Procurement of Goods. To the extent
practicable, contracts for these goods would be grouped in bid packages estimated to cost
US$200,000 equivalent or more. This procurement would be-in accordance with methods, principles
and documents that have already been tested and carried out under the Second Secondary Education
and Management Project (Loan 31 58-IND). These principles include: (a) involving the end-users in
the preparation of the equipment lists for their schools; and (b) making the suppliers of the equipment
responsible for distributing the equipment to the end-users. Any further lessons learned from the
ongoing bid evaluation and forthcoming contract performance would be incorporated into future
bidding documents under the proposed project. Under ICB bidding, local manufacturers would be
granted a margin of preference of 15 percent of the CIF price, or the import taxes and duties which a
non-exempt importer would have to pay, whichever is lower. Contracts costing less than $200,000
up to an aggregate of US$4.0 million including taxes could be procured through NCB procedures
acceptable to the Bank. Small and diverse items, costing less than US$50,000 and totaling US$5.1
million may be procured through national shopping based on at least three price quotations.
a/ This would include about 80 percent of contract awards under NCB procedures and about 10 percent spot-
checking of contracts awarded following simplified procurement procedures with three price quotations. About
one half of this review would be done through desk-reviews in Jakarta. Spot-checks and reviews of noted
irregularities would necessitate field reviews for the remaining 50 percent. RSI would carry out a second review
of about 20 percent of the work of the procurement consultant.



Staff Appraisal Report                           47
4.16   Because of their availability from  single sources only, books and earlier published
educational materials, totaling US$16.9 million, would, with the Bank's prior agreement, be procured
from publishers or local distributors through direct contracting. Printing services for project-specific
educational materials costing less than US$50,000 each and aggregating less than US$2.5 million
would be procured through national shopping with at least three price quotations. There would be no
larger contracts for this purpose. Together these items are estimated to cost a total of US$19.4
million.
4.17   Local training contracts totaling about US$20.5 million would be awarded to local providers
in accordance with Government administrative procedures, acceptable to the Bank.
4.18   Overseas training contracts totaling about US$1.4 million would be awarded in accordance
with Government administrative procedures, acceptable to the Bank.
4.19   Consultant services and studies totaling about US$6.6 million would be selected and
contracted in accordance with Bank guidelines on the use of consultants. About US$3.6 million of
this would be for the provinces, the balance (US$3.0 million) for project management and
supervision from Jakarta. Annex 19 shows the technical assistance plan. All training and consultancy
contracts would use the standard form of contract for consultants' services.
4.20   Scholarships totaling about US$16.1 million would be awarded in accordance with agreed
criteria in accordance with Government administrative procedures, acceptable to the Bank.
4.21   Contract teachers totaling about US$4.7 million would be selected in accordance with
the criteria and procedures agreed with the Bank.
4.22 Operating costs totaling about US$4.9 million would be met following Government
administrative procedures, acceptable to the Bank.

be a large number of small to medium size contracts for goods and works (<US$200,000) to be
awarded under the proposed project:

Kalimantan and West Kalimantan would be subject to prior review. The prior

post review. Review arrangements for construction are also discussed as a part of
the organizational arrangements for construction (para 4.11).
(b)    All terms of reference, single-source selection of consulting firms and all consultant
contracts valued at or over $100,000 for firms and at or over $50,000 for individuals

subject to random ex-post reviews.
(c)    Fellowships contracts would be subject to random ex-post reviews; and Annual
Implementation Plans and Budgets would be subject to annual reviews and
discussions between the Government and the Bank.
4.25   There would be no contract exceeding US$5 million under this project. For contracts not
subject to prior review, post review of such contracts would be carried out by RSI staff
supported by the procurement consultant in PCU (para 4.11). For new implementing agencies,
one out of five such contracts would be post-reviewed for the first year of implementation,
followed by one out of 20 such contracts in succeeding years. Annex 20 shows the procurement
plan for the project by province.

more than US$50,000 and consulting firms contracts valued at or more than US$100,000. All other

documentation would be retained by each managing provincial office and made available for review
by visiting reviewers from Dikmenum and the Bank. To facilitate disbursements, a Special Account
in the amount of US$5.0 million equivalent would be established in Bank Indonesia. This Special

following established procedures. The Special Account would be used for all payments authorized

monthly basis or when 20 percent of the initial deposit has been used, whichever occurs first.
E. ACCOUNTS AND AUDITS
4.28   Based on submissions in an agreed standard format from each province, the CPCU in
Dikmenum would prepare a consolidated financial report. The provincial financial statements and
financial reports for each fiscal year would be audited by independent auditors acceptable to the
Bank, including a separate audit opinion on SOE expenditures. Certified copies of the financial
report on each provincial sub-project for each Government fiscal year, together with the auditors'
statements would be furnished to the Bank as soon as available, but not later than six months after the
end of each Government fiscal year starting March 31, 1997. During negotiations, the Government
provided assurances that it would (i) maintain adequate project records and accounts; (ii) have them
audited each year; (iii) furnish to the Bank the audit report not later than six months after the end of
the fiscal year;and (iv) furnish to the Bank other requested information concerning records, accounts
and audits.
4.29   Dikmenum  has the primary responsibility to monitor all project activities and routine
monitoring would be done through annual reviews. However, because a large number of contracts
would be financed under SOE procedures, an independent, qualified firm with external technical
assistance would be hired to conduct technical audits of key project activities. The above audits
would cover activities in progress and post reviews of completed actions to examine whether
implementation of the various project components are technically appropriate and to recommend
corrective measures if necessary. The procurement oversight activities referred to in para 4.11 would
form an input into these technical audits. During negotiations, the Government gave assurances that
it would (i) maintain adequate records and accounts for all project expenditures made on the basis of
statements of expenditure, (ii) retain records concerning those expenditures for at least one year after
the Bank has received the final project audit report; (iii) allow the Bank to examine such records;
and (iv) ensure the annual audit contains a separate opinion on these records and accounts.



52                  Central Indonesia Junior Secondary Education Project
5. BENEFITS AND RISKS
A. BENEFITS
5.1    The benefits of the proposed project derive from  meeting the high demand for junior
secondary education where it exists and stimulating demand where it may be constrained due to low
income and/or credit market failures. The expected outcomes of the investment are: (a) increased
numbers of higher quality junior secondary graduates; (b) more students at higher levels of education
as approximately 80 percent of junior secondary graduates proceed to the next level and another 30
percent continue to tertiary education; (c) poverty reduction and greater distribution gains in the
economy as a result of focused junior secondary education improvement for low income and rural
students; (d) externalities derived from girls' education such as lower fertility, increased prenatal care
utilization, their families' improved health and nutrition status and children's higher educational
attainment; (e) greater professional qualification of teaching and managerial staff; and (f) better
quality data on students' and schools' performance.
5.2    Outcomes (a) and (b) above are generated as a result of the increased access and enhanced
quality that the project investment will ensure. Table 5.1 below presents the stream of beneficiaries
as a result of access and quality interventions over the next ten years (see Annex 7, Section IV for
details).
Table 5.1: Number of Beneficiaries of Project Investments
(stream of beneficiaries over next 10 years)
Component    Central Java     Yogpakarra  Central Kalimantan    West Kalimantan   Total Project
Access            66,534          3,221            S5,870            10,194        85,819
Quality          426,801         110,081           45,536            95,327       677,745
Total            493,335         113,302           51,406           105,521       763,564
Impact on Poverty
5.3    The project will contribute to offsetting the geographical and financial obstacles barring
poorer children from junior secondary schools, thus reducing social inequality in education access.
Those not enrolled in junior secondary today are predominantly from the lowest four income deciles.
As described in Chapter 3, the project will reach these poor and near-poor through its poverty-
targeted sub-components. Scholarships will be awarded to poor (and capable) students. Schools will
be built in rural areas, with priority given to the poorest villages. Additional places in alternative
programs, which are free to parents, will benefit areas where direct and opportunity costs are highest.
In all, the project will contribute to offsetting the geographical and financial obstacles barring poorer
children from junior secondary schools, thus reducing social inequality in education access.



StaffAppraisal Report                               53
Impact on Women
5.4    The following components can be expected to improve female access to junior secondary
schools: (a) expansion of school places in rural areas, where female participation is lowest;
(b) significant expansion of the SMP Kecil in rural communities in the Kalimantan provinces;
(c) expansion of SMP Terbuka programs in Kalimantan and Central Java; (d) scholarships to low-
income students, with priority given to girls; and (e) quality improvements at private schools,
including Madrasahs which usually has a higher female enrollment.
5.5    Although these actions can be expected to continue closing the remaining gaps between male
and female participation, progress should be monitored throughout the life of the project.
Impact on the Environment
5.6    The project would have no direct environmental effects and is classified as a Category C
project. The proposed project would positively effect the environment indirectly through greater
emphasis on environmental concerns in teacher training, the content of library books, and the
development of science education that makes students more aware of their natural world.
B. RISKS
5.7    The main risks associated with the proposed project are:
(a)    Private sector participation in junior secondary may decline in absolute or relative
terms which would have significant budgetary implications for the public sector. If
the share of the private sector in junior secondary enrollment declines but remains at
its current absolute level of about 3 million students (which means a decline in
relative share from the current 40 percent to about 20 percent), the budgetary shortfall
in the next five years would be twice the shortfall if the private sector retains its 40
percent share (see Annex 7, Section 1I for details). Furthermore, if private enrollment
is phased out (except for enrollment in religious schools), the budgetary shortfall in
the next five years would be three times the level of shortfall if the private sector
continues its significant role. The risk of the decline in the private sector participation
is partly mitigated by the involvement of project advisory committees at all levels
which represent stakeholders interests. This risk would also be mitigated by the
involvement of project steering committees at the central and provincial levels, and
the participation of the MOEC Directorate for private education in these committees.
(b)    The quality improvement component of the project may prove to be more costly and
take longer than initially planned, thus postponing or reducing its expected benefits.
The risks from the labor market side lie in not rewarding higher quality graduates
with a sufficiently high wage premium to generate the estimated economic rate of
return of the quality component of the project: the base case estimate of the economic
rate of return assumes that higher quality graduates will reap a 15 percent additional



54                 Central Indonesia Junior Secondary Education Project
wage premium in the labor market. This generates a rate of return to quality ranging
from 16.5 for Yogyakarta to of 23 percent for West Kalimantan (see Annex 7, Section
IV). Sensitivity analysis indicates that a 10 percent (versus 15 percent) quality wage
premium will reduce the rate of return to 18 percent in West Kalimantan.
(c)   Implementation by PPIUs in the provincial and district offices operating in an
institutional environment still in transition to less centralized procedures. The issue
is to what extent local management can successfully implement these provincial
projects when budgets and all incentive systems, particularly in personnel
management, are under central control.
(d)    The lack of, or limited capacity at the provincial and district offices to manage all
aspects of the project implementation process without previous experience with Bank
requirements and procedures.
5.8    The proposed project would reduce the risks of
(a)    private decline by re-inforcing the mechanisms for school mapping in the project
provinces, so as to avoid construction of public schools in the catchment areas of
existing private schools, and by providing support to private schools (teacher training,
library books and teaching aids) to increase their role in the system and including the
private education representatives in the project advisory committees;
(b)    slower quality improvement by using training programs which have already a long,
positive track-record and supporting further improvement of these programs (such as
PKG for teacher training and the MOEC program for teacher-librarian training); and
by providing the PPIUs with detailed Terms of Reference;
(c)    ongoing deconcentration by confirming assurances from the Government that it is
committed towards the deconcentration of responsibilities, and that it will set up:
(a) PPIUs in each project province to manage the project at provincial level in
coordination with Kandeps (district offices); and (b) a Central Program Coordination
Unit designed to provide technical assistance and to monitor and assess project
implementation.
(d)   insufficient implementation experience by providing assistance (launch seminar and
training, distribution of project implementation handbooks, coordination by CPCU,
national and international consultants) to the provincial PPIUs; establishing criteria
for inter- and intra-provincial equitable distribution of poverty-targeted components;
and provision of regular feedback from World Bank supervision missions.
C.    SUSTAINABILITY
5.9    The fiscal impact of the project on the four provinces is estimated below (see Annex 7,
Section II for details). The estimates are based on the following assumptions: (a) the education budget
in each province will increase at the national historical growth rate of GDP (6 percent per annum in



Staff Appraisal Report                               55
real terms); and (b) the share of the junior secondary education budget out of the total provincial
education budget would also have to increase during the capital-intensive phase.
Central Java
5.10   Based on the above assumptions, total project costs for Central Java (including physical and
price contingencies, and taxes) would account for about 6 percent of the Central Java education
budget, on average, during the project period (1996/97-2001/02). Incremental recurrent expenditures,
which are negligible in the first year of the project, increase from US$0.2 million in 1997/98 to
US$2.3 million in the last year of the project, and stabilize at US$2.5 million after the project is
completed, representing less than 1.0 percent of the education budget. Thus the long-term recurrent
cost implications of the project are small and manageable.
Yogyakarta
5.11   Total project costs for Yogyakarta would account for about 6 percent of the Yogyakarta
education budget, on average, during the project period.  Incremental recurrent expenditures are
negligible because no investment in additional student places will take place in Yogyakarta (other
investments which have recurrent cost implications such as the Management Information System
(MIS) is a very small part of the total).
West Kalimantan
5.12  In West Kalimantan, total project costs would account for about 14 percent of the province's
education budget, on average, during the project period. Incremental recurrent expenditures, which
are negligible in the first year of the project increase from US$0.1 million in 1997/98 to US$0.9
million in the last year of the project, and stabilize at about US$1.0 million after the project is
completed, representing less than 2.0 percent of the education budget. Thus the long-term recurrent
cost implications of the project are manageable.
Central Kalimantan
5.13   Total project costs for Central Kalimantan would account for about 15 percent of the Central
Kalimantan education budget, on average, during the project period.  Incremental recurrent
expenditures, which are negligible in the first two years of the project increase from US$0.14 million
in 1998/99 to US$0.7 million in the last year of the project, and stabilize at about US$0.8 million after
the project is completed, representing less than 2.0 percent of the education budget. Thus the long-
term recurrent cost implications of the project are manageable.
5.14  Institutional sustainability of the project is likely be greatly enhanced because of the
involvement of beneficiaries in project preparation, and by the project design which builds on
successful strategies suited to the variety of contexts in the project provinces. For many interventions
in all provinces under the project, local procedures and programs are being followed and improved to
ensure that implementors are familiar with and committed to the project's design. For example,
inservice teacher training is based on a system that the central MOEC and the provinces have
experience in implementing and that has been carefully designed and tested through years of fine-



56                Central Indonesia Junior Secondary Education Project
tuning. Rather than introducing a new system unfamiliar to provinces and teachers, the project will
focus efforts on improving the existing system. Teacher training will be supported and
institutionalized beyond the life of the project under the auspices of the broad network of MGMP
teacher associations.

6. AGREEMENTS AND RECOMMENDATIONS
A. AGREEMENTS REACHED
6.1    During negotiations, the Government gave assurances that it would:
(a)   employ contract teachers, award scholarships, and select locations for the construction
of schools and dormitories in accordance with criteria agreed with the Bank (para.
3.4);
(b)   establish by November 1, 1996 (i) a Provincial Project Advisory Committee and (ii) a
Provincial Project Implementation Unit in each of the project provinces (para. 3.47);
(c)   establish by November 1, 1996 (i) a Central Program Advisory Committee and (ii) a
Central Program Coordination Unit (para. 3.51);
(d)    not later than October I each year (beginning in 1997), furnish to the Bank for
comments a report on the implementation of the budget for the past and current fiscal
years, and the proposed budget for the following fiscal year (para. 3.52);
(e)   implement policies and procedures to enable adequate project monitoring and
evaluation in accordance with agreed indicators and (i) prepare a report by July 31
each year (beginning in 1997) on monitoring and evaluation activities during the
previous year and recommendations for the following year; (ii) prepare a midterm
report on or about July 31, 1999; and (iii) discuss these reports with the Bank by
October 31 of the same calendar year (para.3.60);
(f)   furnish to the Bank within six months after the project closing date a plan designed to
ensure the continued effectiveness of the project objectives (para. 3.61);
(g)   carry out annual technical audits of project implementation and provide them to the
Bank no later than six months after the end of each fiscal year (para. 3.62).
(h)   maintain adequate project records and accounts; have them audited each year; furnish
to the Bank the audit report not later than six months after the end of each fiscal year;
and furnish to the Bank other requested information concerning records, accounts,
audits (para. 4.28); and
(i)    maintain adequate records and accounts for all project expenditures made on the basis
of statements of expenditure; retain records concerning those expenditures for at least
one year after the Bank has received the final project audit report; allow the Bank to
examine such records; and ensure the annual audit contains a separate opinion on
these records and accounts (para. 4.29).
